Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Nasafi's Proof That Divine Names Indicate Real Attributes
Nasafi proves divine names indicate real attributes through the impossibility of simple negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that divine names are not mere labels but signify real attributes of Allah.

Core Idea
Divine names are not just labels; they affirm real attributes without which statements become contradictory.

Nasafi addresses a central debate in kalam: Do God's names merely label His essence, or do they indicate distinct attributes? He presents a crisp logical argument based on what happens when we negate a name. Let's follow his reasoning and see why the names must be real.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, familiarizing yourself with the flow. Then read the English translation to grasp the argument.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if ... then ...'.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Nasafi's proof targets those who say divine names are merely proper names for the essence, not indicating additional attributes. Identify that claim in the passage.
The opponent's view is implied in the proof's structure.
3
Analyze the conditional
Notice the key conditional: If names were mere labels, then saying 'He is existent but not knowing' would not be contradictory. Why would that be? Reflect on the logic.
4
Consider the consequence
Nasafi argues that if names only affirmed the essence, negating them would negate the essence itself—an absurd result. This shows the names must indicate real attributes.
Think about the difference between negating an attribute and negating existence.
5
Draw the conclusion
Wrap up by stating the conclusion: divine names are established on reality and indicate real meanings, not just the essence.
This refutes the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن هذه الأسامي قررت على الحقيقة وكانت دالة على المعاني وما نقلت عن الوضع الأصلي إلى جعلها أعلاما دالة على الذات فحسب أن يبقى اسم من الأسامي لا يفهم نفي الذات ولم يصر القائل بقوله هو موجود وليس بعالم مناقضا كما يصير بقوله موجود وليس بموجود ولو كان الاسم اسما لإثبات الذات فحسب لا ينفي بنفيه ما يثبت بثبوته فينتفي الذات كما لو قيل ليس بموجود أو ليس بشيء وحيث الأمر على ما قررنا دل ذلك على بطلان كلامهم
And the proof that these names are established upon reality and are indicative of the meanings, and have not been transferred from the original placement to making them proper names indicative of the essence only, is that if a name among the names remains, the negation of the essence would not be understood, and the speaker would not become contradictory by saying 'He is existent and not knowing' as he becomes contradictory by saying 'existent and not existent.' And if the name were only a name for affirming the essence, its negation would not negate what is affirmed by its affirmation, so the essence would be negated—as if it is said 'not existent' or 'not a thing.' And since the matter is as we have established, that indicates the falsity of their statement.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) that the divine names are established upon reality (haqiqa) and indicate real meanings (ma'ani), not merely labels for the essence. Nasafi argues: If these names were merely proper names (a'lam) for the essence, then saying 'He is existent but not knowing' would not be contradictory, just as saying 'existent and not existent' is contradictory. But in fact, the former is considered a contradiction in the context of divine names because it implies a deficiency, whereas the latter is inherently contradictory. If a name like 'knowing' only affirmed existence ('ilm being the essence), then denying it would not remove the essence; it would only negate the label. However, when one says 'He is not knowing,' it is understood as negating a quality, not the essence, but if the name were purely for the essence, saying 'not knowing' would negate the essence itself, which is absurd. Therefore, these names must indicate additional attributes. Nasafi concludes that this demonstrates the falsehood of the opponents' claim that names do not imply attributes.

Key Terms
دليل (dalīl)
حقيقة (haqīqa)
علم ('alam)
مناقضة (munāqaḍa)
نفي (nafy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that negating a divine name like 'knowing' is logically equivalent to negating existence.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if 'knowing' were just a label for existence, then 'not knowing' would mean 'not existent', which is not what is meant. The contradiction arises only because the name indicates a real attribute beyond existence.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does saying 'He is existent but not knowing' become contradictory in the context of divine names?
A) Because 'knowing' is a necessary attribute of God.
B) Because it implies denial of the essence.
C) Because if names were mere labels, denying 'knowing' would not deny existence, but it is considered a deficiency.
D) Because the Qur'an forbids such statements.

Key Takeaway
Divine names signify real attributes because treating them as mere labels leads to logical contradictions in ordinary language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the logical impossibility of reducing Allah's names to mere labels challenge the way you actually use His names in your daily life? Do you invoke them as empty sounds or with a real awareness of the attributes they signify? What will you change today to make your recitation of the Asma' al-Husna more conscious?
?
If you truly believed that every divine name points to a real attribute of Allah, how would that change your understanding of His names during du'a and worship? Will you commit to pausing on each name you utter and reflecting on the attribute it signifies?
?
Consider the contradiction that would arise if Allah's names were just labels. Does your heart feel the weight of affirming that Allah is truly all-Knowing, all-Powerful? Or have you become comfortable with a superficial acknowledgment? What step will you take tonight to deepen that affirmation?

Al-Allaf's Claim: God's Attributes Are His Essence
Nasafi introduces al-Allaf's view identifying attributes with essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf's Mu'tazilite claim that God's attributes are identical to His essence, and distinguish it from the orthodox view of distinct attributes.

Core Idea
Al-Allaf reduces attributes to essence, denying real distinct attributes.

In this passage, al-Nasafi begins his discussion of the Mu'tazilite objections by presenting the view of their leading figure, Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf. Al-Allaf concedes that saying 'God is knowing' affirms knowledge, but he insists that this knowledge is nothing other than God's essence itself. This sets up a key theological dispute about whether attributes are distinct from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note how al-Allaf's view is introduced.
This passage is part of a larger discussion on divine attributes.
2
Identify the attributes mentioned
The passage lists five attributes: knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight. Al-Allaf claims each is identical to God's essence.
3
Understand the core claim
Al-Allaf agrees that calling God 'knowing' affirms knowledge, but he says that knowledge is the essence itself. This denies that attributes are distinct realities.
This view contrasts with the Sunni position that attributes are real and distinct.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why would al-Allaf make this claim? What does it imply about God's oneness? The upcoming refutation will argue against this reduction.
This connects to the lesson objective of distinguishing views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وافترقت المعتزلة في الاعتراض على هذا الكلام فزعم رئيسهم الأعظم أبو الهذيل العلاف أن قولنا إن الله تعالى عالم إثبات للعلم غير أنه يزعم أن علمه ذاته وكذا قوله في قدرته وحياته وسمعه وبصره
And the Mu'tazila differed in objecting to this statement. Their great leader Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf claimed that our saying 'God is knowing' is an affirmation of knowledge, except that he claims that His knowledge is His essence. And likewise his statement regarding His power, life, hearing, and sight.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section titled 'Corruption of the Statement of al-Allaf' (Fasad Qawl al-Allaf). Nasafi states that the Mu'tazila split in their objection to the previous argument (that derived names imply attributes). Their chief, Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf, holds a specific position: He agrees that saying 'God is knowing' affirms knowledge, but he claims that this knowledge is identical to the essence (dhat) of God. Similarly, for power (qudra), life (haya), hearing (sam'), and sight (basar), he asserts they are all the essence itself. This view reduces the attributes to the essence, denying any additional eternal attributes. The passage only presents al-Allaf's claim; the refutation that follows in the text is not fully captured in this window, but the title indicates Nasafi intends to refute it.

Key Terms
إثبات العلم
ذات
قدرة
حياة
سمع
بصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Allaf's view is equivalent to the Ash'ari position on attributes.
Correction: Al-Allaf denies distinct attributes, while Ash'aris affirm they are real and distinct from the essence.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf claim about God's knowledge?
A) That God has no knowledge.
B) That God's knowledge is a distinct attribute.
C) That God's knowledge is His essence.
D) That God's knowledge is created.

Key Takeaway
Al-Allaf's claim that God's attributes are His essence reduces them to mere identity, denying the orthodox belief in real, distinct, eternal attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing,' do you truly internalize that His knowledge is a real, distinct attribute, or do you reduce it to a mere synonym for awareness? How might that affect your reverence for His omniscience?
?
Al-Allaf denied distinct attributes to preserve God's unity. Are you ever tempted to overlook the richness of God's attributes because they seem complex or because you want a God you can fully comprehend? What does that reveal about your humility before His majesty?
?
If you lived with the constant awareness that God's power (qudra) is a distinct eternal attribute, not just an aspect of His essence, how would it change the way you turn to Him in moments of helplessness? What concrete action can you take today to remind yourself of that truth?

Proof That Divine Names Indicate Real Attributes
Nasafi argues that divine names indicate real attributes beyond the essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi proves that divine names indicate real attributes beyond the essence, using a logical argument based on the nature of language and the principle of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Divine names are not mere labels; each name affirms a distinct attribute.

This passage contains a crucial argument from Imam Nasafi against the view that God's names are merely synonyms for His essence. Nasafi uses a linguistic principle: words are placed for specific meanings, so divine names each affirm a distinct attribute. He then provides a logical proof: if names only indicated the essence, then saying 'not knowing' would negate the essence, which it does not. Thus, names indicate real attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic principle
The passage begins with a general observation about words: some indicate absolute existence, others specific existents. Nasafi rejects the claim that a word's benefit is limited to affirming the essence; instead, each word benefits by affirming a specific meaning.
This sets up the analogy for divine names.
2
Apply the principle to divine names
Nasafi argues that each divine name adds a specific meaning beyond what another name adds. For example, 'Knowing' affirms knowledge, not just existence.
3
Follow the proof from contradiction
The proof shows that if names were only for the essence, then negating 'knowing' would negate the essence (since the name only signifies essence), but we don't see that. So the names must indicate real attributes.
This is the core argument.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that the opposing view is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعموم الموضوعة بعضها لمطلق الوجود، وبعضها لموجود مخصوص فلا تدعي أن حصول الفائدة بكل لفظ يدل على ثبوت معنى وراء الذات، بل نقول هناك زيادة الفائدة بكل لفظ لدلالته على ما وضع له كل لفظ، من إثبات زيادة خصوص في الموجود وفيما نحن فيه زيادة الفائدة أيضا لكل لفظ لدلالته على ما وضع له كل لفظ من إثبات معنى وراء ما وضع له اللفظ الآخر لدلالته عليه. وبالوقف على هذه الجملة يعرف فساد الاعتراض. والله الموفق. والدليل على أن هذه الأسامي قررت على الحقيقة وكانت دالة على المعاني، وما نقلت عن الوضع الأصلي إلى جعلها أعلاما دالة على الذات فحسب أن يبقى اسم من الأسامي لا يفهم نفي الذات ولم يصر القائل بقوله : هو موجود وليس بعالم مناقضا، كما يصير بقوله: موجود وليس بموجود، ولو كان الاسم اسما لإثبات الذات فحسب لا ينفي بنفيه ما يثبت بثبوته، فينتفي الذات – كما لو قيل ليس بموجود، أو ليس بشيء – وحيث الأمر على ما قررنا، دل ذلك على بطلان كلامهم.
And the general [nouns] placed, some for absolute existence, and some for a specific existent, so do not claim that the benefit in every word is that it indicates the affirmation of a meaning beyond the essence. Rather we say: there is additional benefit in every word due to its indication of what each word is placed for, of affirming an additional particularity in the existent. And in what we are in, there is also additional benefit for every word due to its indication of what each word is placed for, of affirming a meaning beyond what the other word is placed for, due to its indication. And by stopping at this sentence, the corruption of the objection becomes known. And God is the giver of success. And the proof that these names are established in reality and were indicative of meanings, and that they were not transferred from their original placement to being made proper names indicating only the essence, is that there remains no name among the names for which the negation of the essence is not understood, and the one who says 'He is existent and not knowing' does not become contradictory, as he becomes when he says 'existent and not existent'. And if the name were a name for affirming the essence only, its negation would not negate what it affirms by its affirmation, so the essence would be negated—as if it were said 'not existent' or 'not a thing'. And since the matter is as we have established, that indicates the falsehood of their statement.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the divine names (e.g., knowing, powerful) indicate real attributes distinct from the essence, and are not mere markers of the essence. The author begins by stating a general principle: words are placed for meanings; some are for absolute existence, some for specific existents. He then refutes a potential objection that the only benefit of a word is to affirm the essence. He argues that each word has an additional benefit: it affirms a specific meaning. In the case of divine names, each name adds a meaning beyond what another name adds. For example, 'Knowing' and 'Powerful' each affirm a specific attribute. Reflecting on this reveals the corruption of the opposing view. He then provides a proof: if names were only markers of the essence, then negating a name (e.g., saying 'He is not knowing') would not negate an attribute but only the essence, because the name only signifies the essence. But in fact, we see that saying 'He is existent and not knowing' is not a contradiction, whereas 'He is existent and not existent' is. This shows that 'knowing' is not just another word for the essence; it indicates an attribute. If it were only for the essence, then negating it would negate the essence, similar to negating 'existent'. But it does not; therefore the names indicate real attributes. This proves that the Mu'tazilite position that names are mere markers of the essence is false.

Key Terms
أسامي
ذات
وضع
نفي
إثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine names are mere synonyms for God's essence.
Correction: This passage proves they indicate distinct attributes; otherwise negating a name would negate the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does negating 'knowing' (عالم) not negate the essence?
A) Because 'knowing' is a synonym for the essence.
B) Because 'knowing' indicates a real attribute beyond the essence.
C) Because negation of names is impossible in God's case.
D) Because the essence cannot be negated.

Key Takeaway
Divine names are not mere synonyms for God's essence but indicate real, distinct attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names (e.g., Al-'Aleem, Al-Qadeer), do you truly believe He possesses those attributes in reality? Or do you treat the names as empty titles? How would your du'a change if you affirmed that each name points to a real attribute?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that names are just labels for the essence. Are there any areas in your life where you reduce God's attributes to mere concepts without feeling their reality? What would it mean for your tawhid to truly believe that His names indicate real, distinct attributes?
?
Do you ever find yourself comfortable with a vague belief in God's existence but negligent of His specific attributes? How does this passage challenge that comfort, and what specific attribute of Allah will you meditate on today in your prayer?

Abu Hudhayl's View and the First Objection
Nasafi presents Abu Hudhayl's identity claim and the first refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hudhayl's claim that God's attributes are identical to His essence, and the first objection that this leads to the blasphemous consequence that knowledge must be worshipped.

Core Idea
If attributes are the essence, then God is knowledge, and knowledge becomes an object of worship.

This lesson examines the Mu'tazilite position of Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf, who argued that God's attributes (knowledge, power, life, etc.) are identical to His essence. Nasafi presents the first refutation: if knowledge is the essence, then the essence is knowledge, which would require worshipping knowledge—a blasphemous conclusion. We'll explore this reasoning and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Note the key claim and the objection.
The passage contains an incomplete list of names—focus on the main argument.
2
Identify Abu Hudhayl's claim
Abu Hudhayl asserts that saying 'God is knowing' affirms knowledge, but he claims that knowledge is the essence itself. The same applies to power, life, hearing, and sight.
3
Analyze the first objection
Nasafi argues: if knowledge equals the essence, then the essence is knowledge, so knowledge must be worshipped. He declares that anyone who claims God's knowledge is worshipped is a disbeliever.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: an unacceptable consequence.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why identifying attributes with the essence leads to shirk (worship of something other than Allah). The objection shows that the Mu'tazilite position undermines tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وافترقت المعتزلة في الاعتراض على هذا الكلام فزعم رئيسهم الأعظم أبو الهذيل العلاف أن قولنا: إن الله تعالى عالم إثبات للعلم. غير أنه يزعم أن علمه: ذاته. وكذا قوله في قدرته وحياته وسمعه وبصره. مايدل على معنيين مثل البديع بمعنى صفات ذات وبمعنى المبدع للشيء صفة فعل ومنها الجبار والجميل... إلخ. وهذا الاعتراض فاسد من وجوه أحدها: أن علمه لو كان هو ذاته لكان ذاته علما، فيجب أن يعبد علمه. أن من زعم أن علم الله تعالى يعبد فهو كافر.
And the Mu'tazila differed in objecting to this speech, and their supreme leader Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf claimed that our saying 'God is knowing' is an affirmation of knowledge. But he claims that His knowledge is His essence. And likewise his statement regarding His power, life, hearing, and sight. What indicates two meanings, like al-Badi' (the Incomparable) meaning attributes of essence and meaning the originator of things, an attribute of action, and among them al-Jabbar (the Compeller) and al-Jamil (the Beautiful) ... etc. And this objection is invalid from several aspects. One of them: that if His knowledge were His essence, then His essence would be knowledge, so His knowledge must be worshipped. And whoever claims that God's knowledge is worshipped is a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage introduces the position of Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf, a leading Mu'tazilite, who claimed that God's attributes (knowledge, power, life, etc.) are identical to His essence. The author then presents the first objection against this view. The objection is: if knowledge is the essence, then the essence is knowledge; consequently, that knowledge would be worthy of worship. But it is blasphemous to claim that God's knowledge is worshipped. Therefore, the identity claim leads to an absurd and blasphemous consequence. The passage also includes a parenthetical remark about names like al-Badi' that can indicate two meanings (attribute of essence and attribute of action), but that remark appears incomplete in the OCR.

Key Terms
أبو الهذيل العلاف
المعتزلة
علم
ذات
عبادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Abu Hudhayl's view is a legitimate way to affirm God's attributes without multiplicity, by identifying them with the essence.
Correction: This objection shows that such identity leads to an absurd and blasphemous consequence: if knowledge is the essence, then the essence becomes an object of worship, which contradicts tawhid.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if knowledge is identical to the essence, according to Nasafi's first objection?
A) Knowledge becomes eternal.
B) The essence becomes knowable.
C) Knowledge must be worshipped.
D) All attributes become identical.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hudhayl's identification of attributes with the essence is refuted because it leads to the blasphemous conclusion that God's knowledge must be worshipped.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You call yourself a monotheist, but have you ever unconsciously elevated a single attribute of Allah—His knowledge, His power—in your mind to the point where you think of that attribute almost as a separate deity? This lesson warns that even a theologian's error can lead to shirk. Examine your daily worship: is your love, fear, and hope directed solely to Allah, or do you find yourself worshipping a concept like 'knowledge' or 'mercy' instead of the All-Knowing, the All-Merciful?
?
Abu Hudhayl was the 'supreme leader' of his school, yet his reasoning led to a blasphemous conclusion. Whom do you trust in matters of faith? Are you willing to question a beloved teacher or scholar if their teaching contradicts the Qur'an and Sunnah? This lesson demands that you not accept any claim—even from a respected authority—without testing it against revelation and sound reasoning.
?
The refutation in this lesson shows that a simple identity statement about God's attributes can lead to kufr. How carefully do you think about the words you use when describing Allah? Do you ever casually say 'Allah's mercy' or 'Allah's knowledge' as if these are separate from Him? What steps will you take today to ensure your speech about Allah is always respectful and accurate, so that you do not fall into a similar error?

Second Objection: Knowledge Cannot Subsist in Itself
Nasafi presents the second objection that if knowledge is the essence, it cannot subsist in itself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the identity claim of knowledge and essence leads to a logical contradiction regarding subsistence, and how the opponent's attempted escape by distinguishing reference from concept fails.

Core Idea
If knowledge is identical to the essence, then the essence must subsist in itself, which is impossible.

This lesson examines the second objection raised against Abu Hudhayl's claim that knowledge is identical to the divine essence. Nasafi argues that if knowledge is the essence, then the essence cannot subsist in itself — an impossibility. He then refutes the counter-argument that tries to evade this by claiming that 'knowledge is the essence but the essence is not knowledge,' using the analogy of 'the face of the matter.' The passage is a crisp logical refutation that reveals the inherent contradiction in the opponent's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the original Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get the full argument in the author's own words. Notice the logical structure and key terms.
Pay special attention to the terms قيام (subsistence) and محال (impossible).
2
Understand the initial impossibility claim
Nasafi begins by stating that if the essence were knowledge, it would be impossible for knowledge to subsist in itself. Grasp why this is a problem: knowledge normally requires a subject in which it subsists.
This is the core objection: identity leads to self-subsistence of knowledge, which is impossible.
3
Examine the opponent's attempted escape
Some opponents claim that knowledge is the essence but the essence is not knowledge — an apparent distinction between reference and concept. Nasafi introduces their analogy: 'the face of this matter is such.'
Think about how 'face' can refer to the matter itself yet not be identical in every sense.
4
Follow Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi rejects this as 'making the impossible possible by the impossible.' He explains that 'face' means 'path,' not the essence. If it were the essence, then the matter would be its own face, which is contradictory.
The refutation shows that the analogy does not support the opponent's claim; it actually undermines it.
5
Synthesize the key takeaway
Conclude that any attempt to maintain identity while denying full equivalence fails. The passage demonstrates the necessity of logical consistency in theological discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآخر: وقد صرح أن ذاته لو كان علما لكان يستحيل قيامه بذاته إذ قيام العلم بذاته يستحيل فزعم بعضهم أن علمه ذاته ولكن ذاته ليس بعلم كما يقال: وجه هذا الأمر كذا، فيكون وجه الأمر هو الأمر، والأمر لا يكون وجها. وهذا منهم تصحيح المحال بالمحال لأن من المحال أن يكون العلم هو الذات، والذات لا يكون علما، والاشتغال بتصحيح المحال فاسد. ثم نقول : وجه الأمر هو طريقه، وطريقه ليس بذاته، ولو كان وجهه ذاته، لكان ذاته وجها، لأن من المحال الممتنع أن يكون وجه الأمر هو الأمر، ولا يكون الأمر وجها. والله الموفق.
And the other: and he stated that if His essence were knowledge, it would be impossible for it to subsist in itself, because the subsistence of knowledge in itself is impossible. So some of them claimed that His knowledge is His essence but His essence is not knowledge, as it is said: 'the face of this matter is such', so the face of the matter is the matter, and the matter does not have a face. And this from them is making the impossible possible by the impossible, because it is impossible for knowledge to be the essence and the essence not to be knowledge, and engaging in making the impossible possible is invalid. Then we say: the face of the matter is its path, and its path is not its essence. And if its face were its essence, then its essence would be a face, because it is absolutely impossible for the face of the matter to be the matter and for the matter not to be a face. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against Abu Hudhayl's identity claim. If knowledge is identical to the essence, then since knowledge cannot subsist in itself (it must subsist in a subject), the essence would have to subsist in itself, which is impossible. Some opponents tried to escape by claiming that knowledge is the essence but the essence is not knowledge (i.e., they are identical in reference but different in concept). The author refutes this with an analogy: saying 'the face of this matter' does not mean the face is the matter itself; rather, 'face' means the path or aspect. Similarly, if they claim knowledge is the essence but the essence is not knowledge, that is contradictory because if two things are identical, they must be the same in all respects. He concludes that such attempts to reconcile the impossible are invalid.

Key Terms
قيام
محال
وجه الأمر
تصحيح المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that identity of attribute and essence can be maintained by simply distinguishing between reference (knowledge is the essence) and concept (essence is not knowledge), using ordinary language analogies.
Correction: Nasafi shows that such a distinction leads to contradiction: if two things are identical, they cannot differ in any way. The 'face of the matter' analogy actually undermines the opponent's position, as 'face' means 'path,' not the essence itself.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that knowledge is the essence but the essence is not knowledge?
A) Because it is a theological mystery beyond reason.
B) Because it involves making the impossible possible by the impossible.
C) Because it contradicts divine simplicity.
D) Because it leads to polytheism.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the identity claim by showing that the 'face of the matter' analogy fails to resolve the logical contradiction, thus the impossibility of knowledge subsisting in itself remains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you confront a logical difficulty in your understanding of Allah's attributes, do you tend to resort to mental distinctions like the opponent's 'knowledge is essence but essence is not knowledge' to escape discomfort? What would it cost you to instead admit the limitation of human reason and trust Allah's transcendence?
?
Nasafi dismisses the 'face of the matter' analogy as an invalid attempt to reconcile contradictions. Are there analogies you yourself use in your religious thinking that might be just as flawed? How can you develop the courage to abandon them even if they feel comforting?
?
The opponent's move is to separate the concept from the reality (reference) to avoid an impossible conclusion. In your own life, when do you separate what you say you believe from how you actually live, creating a similar inconsistency? What would it mean for you to make your faith logically coherent in action?

The Third Objection: Attributes Become Identical
Nasafi presents the third objection: identifying attributes with essence makes distinct attributes identical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against Abu Hudhayl's identification of attributes with the essence, and how he is refuted by his own argument against the dualists.

Core Idea
If distinct attributes are each the essence, they become identical.

Nasafi presents a powerful objection: if both knowledge and power are identical to Allah's essence, then they must be identical to each other. Yet they are distinct attributes. This contradiction is sharpened by recalling that Abu Hudhayl himself used a similar logical move against the Manichaean dualists. Let's see how the tables are turned.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the logical structure of the objection and the supporting analogy.
The passage ends abruptly; the core argument is complete.
2
Identify the objection
Understand the logical form: if A = C and B = C, then A = B. Here, knowledge (A) and power (B) are both said to be the essence (C), so they must be identical.
3
Analyze the supporting analogy
The author cites Abu Hudhayl's own refutation of the dualists: if light and darkness are distinct essences that mix, the mixture is not something additional. This shows that if attributes are the essence, they cannot remain distinct.
4
Reflect on the irony
Abu Hudhayl used this logic against others, but now it is turned against his own position. This highlights the difficulty of identifying attributes with the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن علمه لو كان ذاته وقدرته ذاته لكان علمه قدرته. إذ محال أن يكون علمه ذاته وقدرته ذاته وعلمه غير قدرته، لما فيه من إثبات الذات غيرا لنفسه ولما فيه من إثبات المتغايرات في الأزل. يحققه أن أبا الهذيل ألزم الثنوية فقال : إن النور والظلمة لو كانا متباينين لذاتيهما ثم امتزجا لذاتيهما، ولم يكن الامتزاج معنى وراء الذات بل هو عين الذات،
And the second: that if His knowledge were His essence and His power His essence, then His knowledge would be His power. For it is impossible that His knowledge is His essence, His power is His essence, and yet His knowledge is other than His power, because this would entail affirming the essence as other than itself, and affirming mutual distinctions in pre-eternity. And its verification: that Abu Hudhayl refuted the dualists (Manichaeans), saying: 'If light and darkness were mutually distinct in their essences, then they mixed in their essences, and the mixture was not a meaning beyond the essence but it was the essence itself.'
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against Abu Hudhayl. If both knowledge and power are identical to the essence, then they must be identical to each other. But they are distinct attributes, so this leads to contradiction. The author supports this by noting that Abu Hudhayl himself used a similar argument against the dualists (Manichaeans): if light and darkness are distinct essences that mix, the mixture is not an additional thing but the essences themselves. This analogy shows that if attributes are the essence, then there can be no real distinction between them. The passage is cut off but the point is clear: Abu Hudhayl's own argument refutes his position.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Identifying each attribute with the essence preserves monotheism and avoids multiplicity.
Correction: This objection shows that it leads to the absurd conclusion that distinct attributes become identical, which is contradictory.

Checkpoint

B) The face of a matter.
C) The names of God.
D) The negation of existence.

Key Takeaway
This objection shows that if distinct attributes are each identified with the essence, they become identical, which is contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a deeply cherished belief about Allah, are you willing to trace its logical implications to the end—even if they lead to contradiction—or do you stop short? What would it cost you to follow the argument wherever it leads?
?
This lesson shows that even a great scholar like Abu Hudhayl fell into a logical trap. Where in your own understanding of tawhid might you be holding two propositions that, if fully worked out, would clash? Will you examine them today, or let them remain unexamined?
?
Abu Hudhayl‘s own argument against the dualists was used against him. Is there a truth you use to correct others that, if applied honestly to your own position, would expose an inconsistency? Are you ready to be corrected by the same logic you apply to others?

First Refutation: Knowledge as Essence Leads to Worship
Nasafi presents two refutations of the identity of God's knowledge and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's knowledge is identical to His essence leads to absurdity and is refuted by Nasafi.

Core Idea
If knowledge were essence, the essence would be knowledge and thus worshipped – an impossibility.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a central Mu'tazilite claim – that God's knowledge is nothing but His essence. He shows that this position collapses into absurd logical consequences. We will follow his argument step by step, examining both the direct implication and a flawed analogy used to defend the position.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the author first states the objection is invalid, then gives two specific reasons.
The passage uses terms like الذات (essence) and العلم (knowledge).
2
Understand the first refutation
The first argument: if knowledge were essence, then essence would be knowledge, leading to the impossible conclusion that knowledge must be worshipped. Identify why this is impossible according to the author.
The author references a statement by Abu al-Hudhayl that claims worshipping knowledge is disbelief.
3
Analyze the 'face of the matter' analogy
Some defenders claimed that though knowledge is the essence, the essence is not knowledge, using an analogy about the 'face' of a matter. The author refutes this by clarifying that 'face' means 'way', not essence.
The author calls this 'correcting the impossible by the impossible'.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Notice how the author uses both direct consequence and analogical argument to refute the claim. Consider why this refutation is important for the broader discussion of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الاعتراض فاسد من وجوه أحدها: أن علمه لو كان هو ذاته لكان ذاته علما، فيجب أن يعبد علمه. وقد صرح أن من زعم أن علم الله تعالى يعبد فهو كافر. والآخر أن من زعم أن ذاته لو كان علما لكان يستحيل قيامه بذاته إذ قيام العلم بذاته يستحيل فزعم بعضهم أن علمه ذاته ولكن ذاته ليس بعلم كما يقال: وجه هذا الأمر كذا، فيكون وجه الأمر هو الأمر ، والأمر لا يكون وجها. وهذا منهم تصحيح المحال بالمحال لأن من المحال أن يكون العلم هو الذات، والذات لا يكون علما، والاشتغال بتصحيح المحال فاسد. ثم نقول : وجه الأمر هو طريقه، وطريقه ليس بذاته، ولو كان وجهه ذاته، لكان ذاته وجها، لأن من المحال الممتنع أن يكون وجه الأمر هو الأمر، ولا يكون الأمر وجها. والله الموفق.
This objection is invalid in several respects. One: If His knowledge were His essence, then His essence would be knowledge, so it would be necessary to worship His knowledge. And he has declared that whoever claims that the knowledge of God the Exalted is worshipped is an unbeliever. Another: Whoever claims that if His essence were knowledge, it would be impossible for it to subsist in itself, for the subsistence of knowledge in itself is impossible. So some of them claimed that His knowledge is His essence, but His essence is not knowledge, as it is said: 'the face (wajh) of this matter is such,' so the face of the matter is the matter itself, but the matter does not have a face. This from them is correcting the impossible by the impossible, because it is impossible for knowledge to be the essence and the essence not to be knowledge, and occupying oneself with correcting the impossible is futile. Then we say: The face of a matter is its way, and its way is not its essence. If its face were its essence, then its essence would be a face, because it is absolutely impossible for the face of a matter to be the matter itself while the matter is not a face. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God's knowledge is identical to His essence (i.e., the Mu'tazilite position of Abu al-Hudhayl). The author begins by stating that this objection is invalid from multiple angles. The first angle is presented here: if God's knowledge were His very essence, then His essence would be knowledge. But since knowledge is something that can be worshipped (in the sense of being a created thing that leads to worship? Actually, the argument here is that if knowledge were the essence, then the essence would be knowledge, and since knowledge is a thing that could be conceptually distinguished, it would require worship of that knowledge, which is impossible. The author notes that Abu al-Hudhayl explicitly said that whoever claims that the knowledge of God is worshipped is an unbeliever. Another aspect: some claimed that even if knowledge were the essence, the essence is not knowledge, using an analogy of 'the face of a matter' which is the matter itself yet the matter is not a face. The author refutes this as 'correcting the impossible by the impossible', because it is impossible for two things to be identical yet one not have the property of the other. He clarifies that 'the face of a matter' actually means its way or path, not the thing itself. Thus the analogy fails, and the original claim that knowledge is the essence leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
الذات
العلم
الوجه
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy of 'face of the matter' implies that two things can be identical yet one not have the property of the other.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that 'face' means 'way' or 'path', not the essence itself, so the analogy does not support the claim.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would be necessary if God's knowledge were identical to His essence?
A) God would be ignorant.
B) God's knowledge would be worshipped.
C) God would be omnipotent.
D) God's essence would be multiple.

Key Takeaway
The identity of God's knowledge with His essence leads to the absurd conclusion that knowledge must be worshipped, proving the position invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what subtle ways might you be 'worshipping' your own knowledge or intellect rather than the One who bestows it? How can you realign your study of Islam to be an act of worship that points entirely to Him?
?
The refutation shows that knowledge cannot subsist in itself — it depends on God. When you feel self-sufficient in your understanding, do you unconsciously treat your knowledge as an independent power? What practice can you adopt daily to acknowledge that all knowledge is a borrowed gift?
?
The opponents used a flawed analogy (face of a matter) to defend their position. Where in your life are you using a metaphor or analogy to justify a belief that, if examined carefully, falls apart? Will you ask Allah today to expose your self-deceptions and grant you clarity.

The Second Refutation: Attributes Identical to Essence Become Identical to Each Other
Nasafi presents the core argument with two analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues that if divine attributes are identical to the essence, they must be identical to each other, using analogies from earlier theologians.

Core Idea
If knowledge = essence and power = essence, then knowledge = power.

Nasafi continues his refutation of those who identify attributes with the essence. He shows that this position forces absurd consequences: if each attribute is identical to the essence, then all attributes become identical to each other. To illustrate, he invokes two famous arguments from Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Nazzam that demonstrate the logical collapse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the core claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Nasafi begins by stating the premise and its immediate absurd conclusion: that knowledge would equal power.
Notice the phrase 'إذ محال أن يكون علمه ذاته وقدرته ذاته وعلمه غير قدرته'.
2
Examine the first analogy: Abu al-Hudhayl's argument against Dualists
Abu al-Hudhayl argued that if light and darkness are distinct by essence and mix by essence, then mixing and separation become identical. Understand how this parallels the attributes case.
This is a logical analogy, not a physical claim.
3
Examine the second analogy: al-Nazzam's length and width
Al-Nazzam argued that if length is the thing and width is the thing, then length equals width. Similarly, if knowledge is the essence and power is the essence, they become identical.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that this applies to all attributes: life, endurance, hearing, sight. All would collapse into one.
This contradicts the clear scriptural distinction between attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن علمه لو كان ذاته وقدرته ذاته لكان علمه قدرته. إذ محال أن يكون علمه ذاته وقدرته ذاته وعلمه غير قدرته، لما فيه من إثبات الذات غيرا لنفسه ولما فيه من إثبات المتغايرات في الأزل. يحققه أن أبا الهذيل ألزم الثنوية فقال : إن النور والظلمة لو كانا متباينين لذاتيهما ثم امتزجا لذاتيهما، ولم يكن الامتزاج معنى وراء الذات بل هو عين الذات، وكذا التباين كان عين الذات. يجب أن يكون الامتزاج هو التباين، والتباين هو الامتزاج. وألزم النظام في زعمه أن طول الشيء هو الشيء وعرضه هو أيضا أن يكون طوله عرضه وعرضه طوله، فعلى هذا يلزمه أن يكون علمه تعالى قدرته وقدرته علمه، وكذا هذا في الحياة والبقاء والسمع والبصر. وإذا لزم هذا، فقد لزم أن يكون الله تعالى عالما بما به يقدر وقادرا بما به يعلم وهو حال في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب، لوجوب التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب في الممكنات والممتنعات، ولأن العلم لو كان هو القدرة، لكان ما تعلقت به قدرته معلوما، لتعلق ما هو العلم به، وما تعلق به علمه مقدورا لتعلق ما هو القدرة به لاتحاد في المتعلق، وارتفاع في المتعلق، وارتفاع التضاد عن المتعلق به، لتعلق العلم والقدرة بأكثر الأشياء في حالة واحدة من غير استحالة ولا منافاة بين كونه مقدورا وكونه معلوما، وخروج العلم والقدرة من أن يكون من المعاني الإضافية، ويلزم من هذا أن يكون ذات الله تعالى مقدورا له لكونه معلوما له، أو يخرج من أن يكون معلوما له، لاستحالة كونه مقدورا له. وكلا القولين كفر. وكذا أفعال العباد ينبغي أن تكون مقدورة الله تعالى لكونها معلومة له أو لا تكون معلومة لخروجها من أن تكون مقدورة له على أصله. فإن قال بالأول فقد ترك مذهبه في خلق الأفعال. وإن قال بالثاني فقد كفر وإن لم يقل بهما ناقض.
The second: That if His knowledge were His essence and His power were His essence, then His knowledge would be His power. For it is impossible that His knowledge be His essence and His power be His essence yet His knowledge be other than His power, because of establishing the essence as other than itself and because of establishing multiple distinct things in pre-eternity. He makes it clear that Abu al-Hudhayl argued against the Dualists, saying: If light and darkness were distinct by their essences, then they mixed by their essences, and the mixing was not a meaning beyond the essence but was identical to the essence, and likewise separation was identical to the essence. Then mixing must be separation and separation must be mixing. And al-Nazzam argued, in his claim that the length of a thing is the thing itself and its width is also the thing itself, that its length would be its width and its width its length. Accordingly, it follows that His knowledge (exalted) is His power and His power is His knowledge, and likewise with life, endurance, hearing, and sight. And if this follows, then it follows that God the Exalted is knowing by that by which He is powerful and powerful by that by which He is knowing, and this is the case in the witnessed world, so also in the unseen, due to the necessity of equal treatment between the witnessed and the unseen in possible and impossible things. And because if knowledge were power, then whatever His power is connected to would be known, due to the connection of what is knowledge to it, and whatever His knowledge is connected to would be possible, due to the connection of what is power to it, because of unity in the object of connection and lack of opposition in the object of connection, and because knowledge and power are connected to most things in one state without impossibility or contradiction between its being possible and its being known, and the removal of knowledge and power from being relational meanings. And from this it follows that the very essence of God the Exalted would be possible for Him because it is known to Him, or it would cease to be known to Him because it is impossible for it to be possible for Him. Both statements are unbelief. Likewise, the actions of servants must either be possible for God the Exalted because they are known to Him, or not be known because they are not possible for Him according to his principle. If he says the first, he has abandoned his school on the creation of actions. If he says the second, he has disbelieved. And if he says neither, he contradicts himself.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the second refutation of the identification of attributes with essence. The author argues that if both knowledge and power are identical to the essence, then knowledge must be power, which is absurd. He supports this by citing two earlier thinkers: Abu al-Hudhayl's argument against the Dualists (if light and darkness are distinct in essence and mix in essence, then mixing and separation become identical) and al-Nazzam's argument about length and width (if length and width are each the thing itself, they become identical). By analogy, if knowledge and power are each the essence, they become identical. This leads to further absurdities: God would be knowing by that by which He is powerful and powerful by that by which He knows, which is not the case in the witnessed world. Moreover, if knowledge and power are identical, then whatever is known would be possible and vice versa, leading to the implication that God's own essence would be possible (or known) in an impossible way. Both conclusions are unbelief. Additionally, human actions would have to be either possible for God (contradicting the Mu'tazilite denial of divine creation of actions) or not known to Him (amounting to unbelief). The author concludes that this position is self-contradictory.

Key Terms
العلم
القدرة
الذات
الامتزاج
التباين
الطول
العرض
الحياة
البقاء
السمع
البصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Identifying attributes with essence is a harmless simplification.
Correction: Nasafi shows it leads to logical absurdities where attributes become indistinguishable.

Checkpoint
What do the analogies of light/darkness and length/width demonstrate?
A) That attributes are distinct from the essence.
B) That if two things are each identical to a third, they become identical to each other.
C) That mixing and separation are different.
D) That length and width are the same measurement.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi uses logical analogies to show that if attributes are identical to the essence, they become identical to each other, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to claim that God's attributes are identical to His essence, you must also accept that knowledge and power become the same. Would you be comfortable attributing a single, undifferentiated quality to God? Why or why not?
?
This logical argument shows that collapsing attributes into the essence leads to absurdity. How has your own understanding of God's attributes been shaped by intellectual reasoning as opposed to revealed texts? Are you willing to subject your beliefs to such logical scrutiny?
?
The analogy of light and darkness mixing by essence teaches that identity leads to contradiction. Are there areas in your faith where you have uncritically accepted claims without examining their logical implications? What can you do to ensure your beliefs are coherent?

Absurd Consequences: God's Essence and Human Actions
Nasafi draws absurd consequences from the identity of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the further consequences Nasafi draws from the identity of attributes, leading to absurdities about God's essence and human actions.

Core Idea
If knowledge=power, then God's essence would be possible for Him, which is impossible.

Having shown that identifying attributes with essence forces them to become identical, Nasafi now draws out the devastating consequences. If knowledge and power are the same, then whatever is known must be possible, and whatever is possible must be known. This leads to the conclusion that God's own essence would be both known and possible for Him—a contradiction. Furthermore, human actions would either have to be created by God (contradicting Mu'tazilite doctrine) or be unknown to Him (amounting to unbelief).

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting point: knowledge=power leads to unity of objects
If knowledge and power are identical, then their objects (what is known and what is possible) become inseparable. Whatever is known would be possible and vice versa.
Nasafi says 'لاتحاد في المتعلق'.
2
Analyze the consequence for God's essence
Nasafi argues that since God's essence is known to Him, it would also have to be possible for Him, which is impossible because the essence is necessary. This leads to either the essence being possible (unbelief) or not being known (unbelief).
This is a direct attack on the Mu'tazilite position.
3
Analyze the consequence for human actions
Human actions are known to God. If knowledge=power, they would have to be possible for God, meaning God creates them. But Mu'tazilites deny that God creates human actions. So they face a dilemma.
Nasafi concludes that either they abandon their school or fall into unbelief.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Nasafi's refutation shows that the identification of attributes with essence leads to multiple absurdities, making it an untenable position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن علمه لو كان ذاته وقدرته ذاته لكان علمه قدرته. إذ محال أن يكون علمه ذاته وقدرته ذاته وعلمه غير قدرته، لما فيه من إثبات الذات غيرا لنفسه ولما فيه من إثبات المتغايرات في الأزل. يحققه أن أبا الهذيل ألزم الثنوية فقال : إن النور والظلمة لو كانا متباينين لذاتيهما ثم امتزجا لذاتيهما، ولم يكن الامتزاج معنى وراء الذات بل هو عين الذات، وكذا التباين كان عين الذات. يجب أن يكون الامتزاج هو التباين، والتباين هو الامتزاج. وألزم النظام في زعمه أن طول الشيء هو الشيء وعرضه هو أيضا أن يكون طوله عرضه وعرضه طوله، فعلى هذا يلزمه أن يكون علمه تعالى قدرته وقدرته علمه، وكذا هذا في الحياة والبقاء والسمع والبصر. وإذا لزم هذا، فقد لزم أن يكون الله تعالى عالما بما به يقدر وقادرا بما به يعلم وهو حال في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب، لوجوب التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب في الممكنات والممتنعات، ولأن العلم لو كان هو القدرة، لكان ما تعلقت به قدرته معلوما، لتعلق ما هو العلم به، وما تعلق به علمه مقدورا لتعلق ما هو القدرة به لاتحاد في المتعلق، وارتفاع في المتعلق، وارتفاع التضاد عن المتعلق به، لتعلق العلم والقدرة بأكثر الأشياء في حالة واحدة من غير استحالة ولا منافاة بين كونه مقدورا وكونه معلوما، وخروج العلم والقدرة من أن يكون من المعاني الإضافية، ويلزم من هذا أن يكون ذات الله تعالى مقدورا له لكونه معلوما له، أو يخرج من أن يكون معلوما له، لاستحالة كونه مقدورا له. وكلا القولين كفر. وكذا أفعال العباد ينبغي أن تكون مقدورة الله تعالى لكونها معلومة له أو لا تكون معلومة لخروجها من أن تكون مقدورة له على أصله. فإن قال بالأول فقد ترك مذهبه في خلق الأفعال. وإن قال بالثاني فقد كفر وإن لم يقل بهما ناقض.
The second: That if His knowledge were His essence and His power were His essence, then His knowledge would be His power. For it is impossible that His knowledge be His essence and His power be His essence yet His knowledge be other than His power, because of establishing the essence as other than itself and because of establishing multiple distinct things in pre-eternity. He makes it clear that Abu al-Hudhayl argued against the Dualists, saying: If light and darkness were distinct by their essences, then they mixed by their essences, and the mixing was not a meaning beyond the essence but was identical to the essence, and likewise separation was identical to the essence. Then mixing must be separation and separation must be mixing. And al-Nazzam argued, in his claim that the length of a thing is the thing itself and its width is also the thing itself, that its length would be its width and its width its length. Accordingly, it follows that His knowledge (exalted) is His power and His power is His knowledge, and likewise with life, endurance, hearing, and sight. And if this follows, then it follows that God the Exalted is knowing by that by which He is powerful and powerful by that by which He is knowing, and this is the case in the witnessed world, so also in the unseen, due to the necessity of equal treatment between the witnessed and the unseen in possible and impossible things. And because if knowledge were power, then whatever His power is connected to would be known, due to the connection of what is knowledge to it, and whatever His knowledge is connected to would be possible, due to the connection of what is power to it, because of unity in the object of connection and lack of opposition in the object of connection, and because knowledge and power are connected to most things in one state without impossibility or contradiction between its being possible and its being known, and the removal of knowledge and power from being relational meanings. And from this it follows that the very essence of God the Exalted would be possible for Him because it is known to Him, or it would cease to be known to Him because it is impossible for it to be possible for Him. Both statements are unbelief. Likewise, the actions of servants must either be possible for God the Exalted because they are known to Him, or not be known because they are not possible for Him according to his principle. If he says the first, he has abandoned his school on the creation of actions. If he says the second, he has disbelieved. And if he says neither, he contradicts himself.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the second refutation of the identification of attributes with essence. The author argues that if both knowledge and power are identical to the essence, then knowledge must be power, which is absurd. He supports this by citing two earlier thinkers: Abu al-Hudhayl's argument against the Dualists (if light and darkness are distinct in essence and mix in essence, then mixing and separation become identical) and al-Nazzam's argument about length and width (if length and width are each the thing itself, they become identical). By analogy, if knowledge and power are each the essence, they become identical. This leads to further absurdities: God would be knowing by that by which He is powerful and powerful by that by which He knows, which is not the case in the witnessed world. Moreover, if knowledge and power are identical, then whatever is known would be possible and vice versa, leading to the implication that God's own essence would be possible (or known) in an impossible way. Both conclusions are unbelief. Additionally, human actions would have to be either possible for God (contradicting the Mu'tazilite denial of divine creation of actions) or not known to Him (amounting to unbelief). The author concludes that this position is self-contradictory.

Key Terms
المتعلق
المعاني الإضافية
الذات
أفعال العباد
الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Identifying attributes with essence only affects the attributes themselves, not other doctrines.
Correction: Nasafi shows it leads to contradictions about God's essence and human free will, undermining core Islamic beliefs.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does Nasafi present regarding God's essence?
A) Either essence is eternal or created.
B) Either essence is possible for God or not known to God.
C) Either essence is simple or composed.
D) Either essence is one or many.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that identifying attributes with essence forces absurd conclusions about God's essence and human actions, making it an untenable belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that God's attributes are identical to His essence, then do you truly accept that every action you perform—including your sins—is directly created by God? How does this affect your understanding of personal responsibility and repentance?
?
The argument shows that denying God's creation of actions leads to denying His knowledge. Do you live as if God knows every detail of your life, even the moments you wish He didn't see? What would change if you fully internalized that His knowledge of your actions is inseparable from His power over them?
?
Nasafi says that identifying attributes with essence can lead to statements of unbelief. Have you ever held a belief about God that, when logically pressed, forced you into a contradiction you had to resolve? What did that resolution cost you—and did it bring you closer to the truth or to comfort?

Divine Attributes

The Absurdity of Identifying Essence with Attributes: Initial Refutation
Nasafi uses analogical reasoning to reduce al-Nazzam's view to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes al-Nazzam's claim by showing that identifying attributes with essence leads to logical absurdities, such as mixing being identical to distinction.

Core Idea
If attributes are identical to essence, then opposites become the same.

This lesson introduces the first part of Imam Nasafi's refutation of al-Nazzam, who argued that God's attributes are nothing but His essence. Nasafi shows that such a view collapses the basic distinctions of logic and reality, using analogies from length and width, then applying it to divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its literal translation to get the initial flavor of the argument.
The passage uses a concise, logical style typical of kalam.
2
Understand the analogy of mixing and distinction
If distinction is identical to essence, then mixing must be distinction, which is absurd. This sets the pattern for the refutation.
Note that 'distinction' (تباين) and 'mixing' (امتزاج) are opposites in the witnessed world.
3
Apply the analogy to length and width
Al-Nazzam claims that an object's length is the object itself, and its width is the object itself. Then length would equal width, which is impossible.
This absurdity shows the problem of identifying attributes with the essence.
4
Extend to divine attributes
Similarly, if knowledge is the essence and power is the essence, then knowledge must be power and vice versa. This contradicts our understanding of distinct attributes.
The same applies to life, lastingness, hearing, and sight.
5
Reflect on the logical principle
The identity of attributes to essence leads to the identification of distinct properties, which is rationally impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum of al-Nazzam's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا التباين كان عين الذات. يجب أن يكون الامتزاج هو التباين، والتباين هو الامتزاج. وألزم النظام في زعمه أن طول الشيء هو الشيء وعرضه هو أيضا أن يكون طوله عرضه وعرضه طوله، فعلى هذا يلزمه أن يكون علمه تعالى قدرته وقدرته علمه، وكذا هذا في الحياة والبقاء والسمع والبصر. وإذا لزم هذا، فقد لزم أن يكون الله تعالى عالما بما به يقدر وقادرا بما به يعلم وهو حال في الشاهد فكذا في الغائب، لوجوب التسوية بين الشاهد والغائب في الممكنات والممتنعات، ولأن العلم لو كان هو القدرة، لكان ما تعلقت به قدرته معلوما، لتعلق ما هو العلم به، وما تعلق به علمه مقدورا لتعلق ما هو القدرة به لاتحاد في المتعلق، وارتفاع في المتعلق، وارتفاع التضاد عن المتعلق به، لتعلق العلم والقدرة بأكثر الأشياء في حالة واحدة من غير استحالة ولا منافاة بين كونه مقدورا وكونه معلوما، وخروج العلم والقدرة من أن يكون من المعاني الإضافية، ويلزم من هذا أن يكون ذات الله تعالى مقدورا له لكونه معلوما له، أو يخرج من أن يكون معلوما له، لاستحالة كونه مقدورا له. وكلا القولين كفر. وكذا أفعال العباد ينبغي أن تكون مقدورة الله تعالى لكونها معلومة له أو لا تكون معلومة لخروجها من أن تكون مقدورة له على أصله. فإن قال بالأول فقد ترك مذهبه في خلق الأفعال. وإن قال بالثاني فقد كفر وإن لم يقل بهما ناقض. وكثير من أصحابنا من يجعل هذا الكلام ابتداء دليل في المسألة فيقولون لو كان تعالى عالما بنفسه قادرا بنفسه لكان قادرا بما به يعلم وهو محال ويقررون على ما مر وبالله العصمة.
And likewise, distinction is the essence. It must be that mixing is distinction and distinction is mixing. And al-Nazzam is compelled in his claim that the length of a thing is the thing and its width is also that its length is its width and its width is its length. So upon this, it is compelled for him that His knowledge, exalted, is His power and His power is His knowledge, and likewise this in life, lastingness, hearing, and sight. And if this is compelled, then it is compelled that Allah, exalted, is knowing by that by which He is powerful and powerful by that by which He is knowing, and this is the case in the witnessed, so likewise in the unseen, due to the necessity of equivalence between the witnessed and the unseen in the possible and the impossible. And because if knowledge were power, then what His power is attached to would be known, because what is knowledge is attached to it, and what His knowledge is attached to would be powerful because what is power is attached to it, due to unity in the object of attachment and elimination of opposition concerning the object of attachment, because knowledge and power are attached to most things in one state without impossibility or contradiction between it being powerful and it being known, and the exiting of knowledge and power from being among the relational meanings. And from this it is compelled that the essence of Allah, exalted, is powerful for Him because it is known to Him, or it exits from being known to Him, due to the impossibility of it being powerful for Him. And both statements are disbelief. And likewise the actions of servants should be powerful for Allah, exalted, because they are known to Him, or they are not known because they exit from being powerful for Him according to his principle. If he says the first, he has abandoned his school on the creation of actions. And if he says the second, he has disbelieved, and if he does not say both, he contradicts. And many of our companions make this speech a beginning of a proof in the issue; they say: If He, exalted, were knowing by Himself and powerful by Himself, then He would be powerful by that by which He knows, which is impossible. And they elaborate as passed, and with Allah is protection.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Nazzam's view that divine attributes are identical to the essence. The author begins by showing that if attributes were identical, then logically mixing would be the same as distinction, and length would be the same as width—absurdities that show the impossibility of the claim. Then he applies this to the attributes of God: if knowledge were identical to power, then knowledge would be power and vice versa. This would lead to the consequence that God knows by that by which He is powerful and is powerful by that by which He knows, which contradicts the observable realities in the witnessed world where knowledge and power are distinct. Such an equivalence would also necessitate that whatever God knows is also within His power, and vice versa, removing any opposition between the objects of knowledge and power. This would further imply that God's own essence, being known to Him, would have to be within His power, which is impossible because the essence is not an object of power. Both claiming that the essence is powerful for Him or that it is not known to Him are disbelief. The argument then extends to human actions: if knowledge and power are identical, then either human actions are under God's power because He knows them (which would require the Mu'tazila to abandon their doctrine of creation of actions) or they are not known because they are not under His power (which is disbelief). The author concludes by noting that many Maturidi scholars use this very argument as a foundational proof for the distinction between attributes and essence.

Key Terms
الذات
العلم
القدرة
التباين
الامتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Nazzam's view is simply a different opinion that can be tolerated.
Correction: Nasafi shows it leads to logical contradictions, making it rationally untenable.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Nasafi use to illustrate the absurdity of identifying attributes with essence?
A) Analogy of mixing and distinction
B) A verse from the Quran
C) Consensus of scholars
D) A historical anecdote

Key Takeaway
If attributes are identical to essence, then logical opposites collapse, proving the necessity of distinguishing attributes from essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes treat God's attributes as if they conflict with each other—for example, doubting His mercy when facing His justice? This passage shows that attributes are distinct but not contradictory. Will you commit to trusting both His mercy and His justice simultaneously, even when your mind cannot reconcile them?
?
Have you ever oversimplified God's oneness by collapsing His attributes into a single concept to make Him easier to comprehend? This passage warns that such a path leads to logical absurdity. Are you willing to accept the mystery of God's distinct attributes without trying to reduce them to sameness?

The Devastating Consequences: God's Essence and Human Actions
Nasafi deduces theological impossibilities and a dilemma for the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the further consequences of al-Nazzam's identity claim, including the implication that God's essence would be both known and powerful, and the dilemma it poses for the Mu'tazila on human actions.

Core Idea
Identity of attributes leads to the impossible conclusion that God's essence is under His power.

Having shown the initial absurdity, Imam Nasafi now presses further: if knowledge is power, then God's own essence, being known, must also be within His power—an impossibility. He then turns to human actions, exposing the inconsistency of the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Carefully read the Arabic text and translation, noting the logical deductions.
This part builds on the previous conclusion that knowledge equals power.
2
Understand the entailment about God's essence
If knowledge is power, then God knows by His power and is powerful by His knowledge. Then whatever is known must be within His power.
Especially note that God's own essence is known to Him.
3
Grasp the two impossible options
Either God's essence is within His power (impossible) or it is not known to Him (also impossible). Both are disbelief.
This shows the identity thesis leads to kufr.
4
Apply to human actions
Human actions are known to God. If knowledge and power are identical, then either actions are under God's power (Mu'tazila deny this) or actions are not known to God (kufr).
The Mu'tazila are forced into a contradiction or disbelief.
5
Appreciate the Maturidi proof
Nasafi notes that many Maturidi scholars use this very argument as a foundational proof for the distinction between attributes and essence.
The argument is not merely polemical but constructive.

And likewise, distinction is the essence. It must be that mixing is distinction and distinction is mixing. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Nazzam's view that divine attributes are identical to the essence. According to the passage, what would be the consequence if human actions are known to God and knowledge is identical to power?
A) Human actions are not created by God.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever casually held a belief about Allah that, upon closer examination, leads to a logical absurdity? What does this passage teach you about the danger of theological carelessness? Will you commit to verifying your beliefs against sound reasoning from revelation?
?
Nasafi's argument forces a choice between two forms of kufr. In your own life, are there gray areas where you try to avoid choosing between what Allah has commanded and your desires? How does this stark dilemma challenge you to seek the truth uncompromisingly?
?
If you believed that Allah's attributes are not distinct from His essence, would your worship change? Does the distinction protect the majesty of Allah? Reflect on how this doctrine deepens your awe of a Creator whose essence and attributes are beyond your full comprehension.

The Third Argument: If Knowledge Were the Essence, It Would Be Described by All Attributes
Imam Nasafi argues that if knowledge were the essence, knowledge would assume all divine attributes, which is absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying divine knowledge with the essence leads to an absurd consequence according to Imam Nasafi, and how this refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's position.

Core Idea
If knowledge equals essence, then knowledge must be Creator, Living, etc.— which is absurd.

Imam Nasafi presents a third argument against the claim that divine attributes are identical to the essence, specifically targeting Abu al-Hudhayl's school. This argument shows that if knowledge were the essence, it would have to possess all the attributes that the essence does, which is rationally impossible. The passage also hints at objections that Nasafi chooses not to address here.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own logic come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is the third in a series of arguments.
2
Understand the conditional premise
Identify the 'if-then' structure: if knowledge is identical to the essence, and the essence is described as Living, Powerful, Creator, etc., then knowledge must also be described by all those attributes.
3
Recognize the absurd conclusion
The conclusion is that knowledge would have to be the Creator, the Maker, the Manager, etc. This is impossible because attributes are not themselves the subjects of such descriptions.
Nasafi calls this 'a departure from the dictate of intellects.'
4
Note the extension to all attributes
Observe that Nasafi explicitly applies the same reasoning to power, life, hearing, sight, and other attributes. The argument is not limited to knowledge alone.
This refutes the idea that only knowledge is identical to the essence.
5
Consider why Nasafi omits objections
Nasafi acknowledges that opponents have objections but chooses not to mention them here to avoid length, promising that resolving those doubts will become clear when discussing the issue of the Qur'an.
This shows the interconnectedness of theological questions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث: أن علم الله تعالى لو كان هو الذات، والذات هو الحي، الباقي، القادر السميع البصير، الخالق الباري، المصور، المدبر، لكان العلم هو الموصوف بهذا كله. وكذا القدرة والحياة والبقاء والسمع والبصر. وهذا خروج عن قضية العقول. ولهم اعتراضات على ما أفسدنا به مذهب أبي الهذيل تركنا ذكرها كراهية التطويل، ولأن العلم بدفعها يحصل عند الوقوف على حل شبهاتهم في مسألة القرآن - إن شاء الله تعالى.
And the third: That the knowledge of Allah, exalted, if it were the essence, and the essence is the Living, the Everlasting, the Powerful, the Hearing, the Seeing, the Creator, the Maker, the Fashioner, the Manager, then knowledge would be described by all of this. And likewise power, life, lastingness, hearing, and sight. And this is a departure from the dictate of intellects. And they have objections to what we have corrupted the school of Abu al-Hudhayl by; we have omitted mentioning them to avoid length, and because the knowledge of repelling them is attained upon resolving their doubts concerning the issue of the Qur'an—if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument against the claim that divine attributes are identical to the essence, specifically targeting the position of Abu al-Hudhayl. The author states that if God's knowledge were the same as His essence, and the essence is described as Living, Everlasting, Powerful, Hearing, Seeing, Creator, Maker, Fashioner, and Manager, then the attribute of knowledge itself would have to be described by all these names and attributes. The same would apply to all other attributes like power, life, etc. This is an absurdity because attributes are not themselves the subject of such descriptions; only the essence can be described in that way. Such a consequence goes against reason. The author then mentions that there are objections raised by opponents against this refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl's school, but he chooses not to elaborate to avoid lengthening the discussion, and because understanding those objections becomes clear when one studies the resolution of their doubts concerning the issue of the Qur'an's createdness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only applies to knowledge, not other attributes.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states 'And likewise power, life, lastingness, hearing, and sight.' The argument applies to all attributes equally.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Nasafi argue would follow if knowledge were identical to the essence?
A) Knowledge would be eternal like the essence.
B) Knowledge would be described by all the attributes of the essence, such as being Creator.
C) Knowledge would cease to exist.
D) The essence would become knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi refutes identitarianism by showing that if knowledge were identical to the essence, it would become qualified with all divine attributes, which is rationally untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge were the essence, then knowing would be the same as being. In your life, do you sometimes act as if your knowledge of Islam is identical to your actual state of being? In what specific area have you learned the truth but not lived it? What will you change today to bridge that gap?
?
Nasafi shows that reducing the essence to a single attribute is absurd. Consider: Do you ever reduce your own identity before Allah to one quality (e.g., 'I am knowledgeable' or 'I am pious'), neglecting other responsibilities? Confess one attribute you have overemphasized at the expense of others, and commit to restoring balance this week.
?
The argument refutes those who conflate attributes with essence. In your understanding of Allah, have you ever limited His perfection by focusing on one attribute while ignoring others? Which attribute of Allah do you need to reflect on more deeply starting today to correct your view of Him?
