Maturidi Responses on the Eternity of Knowledge
Al-Nasafi presents an objection and three Maturidi views on the eternity of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Maturidi responses to the objection that God's attribute of knowledge is eternal in a problematic sense, and how they preserve tawhid.

Core Idea
The Maturidis avoid saying attributes are separately eternal; Allah is eternal with His attributes.

This passage from al-Nasafi addresses a theological objection: if God's knowledge is eternal (baqi), does that imply a separate eternal entity? The Maturidi school offers three nuanced answers that avoid this pitfall. Understanding these responses clarifies the Maturidi commitment to tawhid and their critique of Abu al-Hudhayl.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting how the objectors frame the question: is affirming the eternity of knowledge affirming an essence or a meaning?
The objectors draw a parallel to the earlier discussion of 'qadim'.
2
Identify the first response
Find the first response: the early Maturidis refrain from saying any attribute is eternal; rather, Allah is eternal with His attributes.
3
Identify the second response
Find the second response: some say knowledge is eternal by a permanence subsisting in God's essence.
4
Identify the third response and refutation
Find the third response: God is eternal by a permanence identical to His essence. Note how this refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's contradictory doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يعترضون أيضا فيقولون: إن صفة العلم عندكم باقية في الغائب أإثبات ذات هذا أم إثبات معنى؟ ويسوقون الكلام على ما ساقوا في القديم. والجواب عنه : أن قدماء أصحابنا يمتنعون عن القول بأن شيئا من صفاته باق، بل يقولون : إن الله تعالى باق بصفاته فلا يتوجه عليهم الإلزام. ومنهم من يقول هذا الاسم إثبات للبقاء، غير أن علمه تعالى باق ببقاء قائم بذات الباري. فهؤلاء أيضا قد تقصوا عن عهدة الإلزام. ومنهم من يقول : هو باق ببقاء هو وذاته، فكان علما للذات بقاء لنفسه، ولا يفسد هذا، وإنما يفسد به مذهب أبي الهذيل، أنه جعل علم الله تعالى ذات الله تعالى كما جعل الذات علما، وهذا يتناقض، وهؤلاء أصحابنا لما جعلوا بقاء العلم نفس العلم جعلوا نفس العلم بقاء. وسنكشف عن حقيقة هذا الكلام إذا انتهينا إلى أجوبة شبهات الخصوم - إن شاء الله تعالى.
And perhaps they also object saying: The attribute of knowledge according to you is eternal (baqiya) in the unseen. Is it an affirmation of an essence of this or an affirmation of a meaning? And they conduct the speech as they conducted regarding 'qadim'. The answer: Our early companions refrain from saying that any of His attributes are eternal, rather they say: Allah is eternal with His attributes, so the objection does not apply to them. And some of them say: This name is an affirmation of permanence, except that His knowledge is eternal by a permanence subsisting in the essence of the Creator. These too have fulfilled the obligation of the objection. And some of them say: He is eternal by a permanence that is He and His essence, so it became a name for the essence, permanence for itself. And this does not corrupt, rather it corrupts the doctrine of Abu al-Hudhayl, who made the knowledge of Allah the essence of Allah, just as he made the essence knowledge, and this is contradictory. And our companions, when they made the permanence of knowledge the same as knowledge, they made knowledge itself permanence. And we will uncover the reality of this speech when we reach the answers to the doubts of the opponents, God willing.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and three Maturidi responses regarding whether God's attribute of knowledge is eternal (baqi). The objectors ask: is the eternity of knowledge an affirmation of an essence or a meaning? They draw a parallel to the earlier discussion of 'qadim'. Al-Nasafi gives three answers from the Maturidi school: 1) The early Maturidis avoid the problem by stating that Allah is eternal with His attributes, not that each attribute is separately eternal. 2) Some say that knowledge is eternal by a permanence subsisting in God's essence. 3) Others say that God is eternal by a permanence identical to His essence, making 'eternal' a name for the essence itself. The third view does not cause harm but rather refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's bid'ah doctrine that equates God's knowledge with His essence, which is contradictory because it makes the attribute identical to the essence and vice versa. The Maturidis instead make the permanence of knowledge identical to knowledge itself. Al-Nasafi promises to clarify further when addressing opponents' doubts.

Key Terms
باق
بقاء
صفة العلم
أبو الهذيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Maturidis affirm separate eternal entities besides God by saying attributes are eternal.
Correction: The Maturidis avoid this by affirming that Allah is eternal with His attributes, not that attributes are separately eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the response of the early Maturidis (qadimat ashabina) to the objection about knowledge being eternal?
A) They affirm that knowledge is eternal as a separate entity
B) They say Allah is eternal with His attributes, so the objection does not apply
C) They deny that knowledge is an attribute
D) They say knowledge is eternal by a permanence distinct from God

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis maintain that Allah is eternal with His attributes, avoiding any implication of multiple eternal entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever inadvertently treated something other than Allah as if it were eternal or permanent? What does this lesson teach you about guarding your heart from such subtle shirk?
?
The Maturidis refrained from saying any single attribute is eternal in isolation. In what area of your life do you need to be more careful to attribute permanence only to Allah, and what practical step will you take to realign your thinking?
?

The Quran Described as Majid: A Metaphorical Interpretation
Al-Nasafi responds to an objection about the Quran being described as 'majid' without a meaning of majesty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi resolves the objection that describing the Quran as 'majid' would imply an attribute subsisting in another attribute by explaining it as a metaphorical expression.

Core Idea
The Quran's description as 'majid' is metaphorical, not literal.

Al-Nasafi anticipates a subtle objection: if an attribute cannot subsist in another attribute, how can Allah describe the Quran as 'majid' (glorious) when that word seems to denote an attribute? He offers a concise answer grounded in the permissibility of metaphorical language in the Quran. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once silently, then read the translation. Notice how the objection is structured and the response begins with 'for we say'.
The Arabic uses 'la yuqalu' (it is not said) to frame the objection.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that describing the Quran as 'majid' is problematic because it would require majesty to be an attribute of the Quran, but attributes cannot subsist in attributes.
The Quran itself is the speech of Allah, which is an attribute.
3
Understand the response
Al-Nasafi clarifies that 'majid' is used metaphorically to indicate majesty, not as a literal attribute. Metaphorical language does not require the same conditions as literal predication.
He distinguishes this from the case at hand (divine attributes).
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction is important in the broader discussion of divine attributes. How does it protect the integrity of the Quran's description without compromising theological principles?
This connects to the broader Sunni view on metaphorical language in scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال إن الله تعالى وصف القرآن بأنه مجيد وإن لم يكن المجد معنى قائما به لاستحالة قيام الصفة بالصفة، لأنا نقول : أراد بالمجيد المجد مجازا، لا مجد لمن يمسك به، ولا كلام في الألفاظ المجازية. وفيما نحن فيه الأمر بخلافه.
And it is not said that Allah described the Quran as majestic (majid) even though majesty (majd) is not a meaning subsisting in it, due to the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in an attribute. For we say: He intended by 'majid' majesty metaphorically, not majesty for the one who holds it. And there is no discussion regarding metaphorical expressions. And in our case here, the matter is different.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: If it is impossible for an attribute to subsist in another attribute, how can the Quran be described as 'majid' (glorious/majestic) if majesty is an attribute? Al-Nasafi answers that this is a metaphorical usage; the term 'majid' is used to indicate majesty metaphorically, not as a real attribute subsisting in the Quran. Metaphorical language is permissible and does not require a literal interpretation. Therefore, this objection does not apply to the subject at hand, which concerns the attributes of God.

Correction: Al-Nasafi explains that this is a metaphorical usage; 'majid' indicates majesty in a figurative sense, not as a real attribute. Metaphorical expressions are permissible and do not require literal subsistence.

Checkpoint

A) As a literal attribute of the Quran
B) As a metaphorical expression
C) As a name for the Quran's essence
D) As a denial of divine attributes

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi resolves the apparent problem of describing the Quran as 'majid' by clarifying that it is a metaphorical expression, not a literal attribute subsisting in the Quran.

Refuting the Claim that God Knows by His Essence
Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that God knows by His essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that God cannot know by His essence and must know by a distinct attribute.

Core Idea
God's essence is unlike accidents; knowledge and ignorance are accidents, so He cannot know by His essence.

Al-Nasafi tackles a key objection from those who claim God knows by His essence rather than by an attribute of knowledge. He uses a comparison between essence and knowledge to show the impossibility of the claim. This passage establishes the necessity of divine attributes as distinct from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the argument's flow and the abrupt ending.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main argument is complete.
2
Understand the initial comparison
Al-Nasafi asks: is there more agreement between God's essence and knowledges, or between knowledge and ignorance? He argues that claiming agreement between essence and knowledge is erroneous because knowledge and ignorance are accidents.
Accidents cannot subsist in God's essence.
3
Follow the a fortiori argument
If it's impossible for God to know by ignorance, it is even more impossible for Him to know by His essence. This strengthens the refutation.
The argument uses logical priority.
4
Examine the counter-argument and response
The opponent suggests that just as God's knowledge differs from ours yet we call it knowledge, so too His essence could cause knowing. Al-Nasafi replies that this is contradictory: if God's knowledge were entirely different from knowledge, it would not be knowledge.
This preserves the nature of knowledge as an attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: الموافقة بين ذات الله تعالى وبين العلوم أكثر ، أم الموافقة بين العلم والجهل؟ فإن :قالوا بين ذاته وبين العلوم أحالوا ، لأن العلم والجهل كل واحد منهما عرض مستحيل البقاء، ومفترق إلى محل وكل واحد منهما في الشاهد من جنس الضمائر، ولا موافقة بين ذات الله تعالى وبين العلم بوجه من الوجوه. ثم لما استحال أن يعلم بالجهل، وتصير الذات به عالمة فلأن يستحيل أن يعلم بذات الله وتصير الذات به عالمة أولى. ولا يقال : إن بين علم الباري وبين علومنا مخالفة ومع ذلك جاز لكم أن تقولوا: إنه عالم بعلم، كما يعلم المحدث بالعلم، وإن لم يكن بين العلم القديم والعلم المحدث موافقة، فكذا جاز لنا أن نقول: إنه يعلم بذاته وإن لم يكن بين ذاته وبين العلم المحدث موافقة قلنا : قولكم: إن علمه مخالف للعلوم، كما أن ذاته تخالفها مناقضة، لأنه علم فلو خالف العلوم لخالف نفسه فإن قالوا: هل يخالف
Then it is said to them: Is the agreement between the essence of Allah and knowledges greater, or the agreement between knowledge and ignorance? If they say: between His essence and knowledges, they would be in error, because both knowledge and ignorance are accidents impossible to subsist, and they are divided into a subject, and each of them in the witnessed world is of the type of accidents, and there is no agreement between the essence of Allah and knowledge in any respect. Then since it is impossible that (He) knows by ignorance, and the essence becomes knowing by it, then it is more appropriate that it is impossible that He knows by the essence of Allah and the essence becomes knowing by it. And it is not said: There is a difference between the knowledge of the Creator and our knowledges, and yet it is permissible for you to say that He knows by knowledge, just as the originated being knows by knowledge, even if there is no agreement between the eternal knowledge and the originated knowledge. So likewise it is permissible for us to say that He knows by His essence, even if there is no agreement between His essence and originated knowledge. We said: Your statement that His knowledge is different from knowledges, just as His essence is different from them, is contradictory, because it is knowledge, so if it were different from knowledges, it would differ from itself. For if they say: Does it differ...
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that God knows by His essence rather than by a distinct attribute of knowledge. Al-Nasafi uses a comparison: between God's essence and created knowledge, or between knowledge and ignorance? If they claim similarity between essence and knowledge, they err because knowledge and ignorance are accidents that cannot subsist in the divine essence. There is no similarity between God's essence and any accident. Then he argues: if it is impossible for God to know by ignorance (which would be absurd), then it is even more impossible for Him to know by His essence. To the counter-argument that just as we accept God's knowledge differs from ours yet we call it knowledge, we could similarly accept God's essence as the cause of knowledge despite difference, Al-Nasafi responds that this is contradictory: God's knowledge is knowledge, so if it were entirely different from knowledge, it would not be knowledge. The passage ends with a question from the opponent: 'Does it differ?' indicating further debate, but the text is cut off.

Correction: Al-Nasafi points out the contradiction: if God's knowledge were entirely different from knowledge, it would not be knowledge. The case of essence is not analogous because essence is not a quality.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi shows that God's essence cannot know by itself because knowledge is an accident, and the essence is not like accidents; therefore, God must know by a distinct attribute.

Different Views on God's Eternity and the Attribute of Baqa'
Nasafi discusses various opinions on whether God is eternal by an attribute or by Himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major opinions on how God's eternity (baqa') is understood and why the Maturidi position avoids the problems of Abu al-Hudhayl's view.

Core Idea
God's eternity is not a separate entity but an attribute identical to His essence.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents three responses to an implied objection about the attribute of eternity. The objection is that if God is eternal by an attribute, that attribute must itself be eternal, leading to an infinite regress. Nasafi shows how different schools of thought answer this, and then critiques the view of Abu al-Hudhayl, clarifying the Maturidi position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the three opinions and the critique.
2
Identify the three opinions
Notice the three groups: those who say God is eternal by His attributes, those who say His knowledge is eternal by an eternity in His essence, and those who say He is eternal by an eternity identical to His essence.
3
Understand the critique of Abu al-Hudhayl
Nasafi explains why Abu al-Hudhayl's view (making knowledge and essence identical in a problematic way) is contradictory.
4
Grasp the Maturidi position
The Maturidis equate the eternity of knowledge with knowledge itself, avoiding a separate attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باق، بل يقولون : إن الله تعالى باق بصفاته فلا يتوجه عليهم الإلزام. ومنهم من يقول هذا الاسم إثبات للبقاء، غير أن علمه تعالى باق ببقاء قائم بذات الباري. فهؤلاء أيضا قد تقصوا عن عهدة الإلزام. ومنهم من يقول : هو باق ببقاء هو وذاته، فكان علما للذات بقاء لنفسه، ولا يفسد هذا، وإنما يفسد به مذهب أبي الهذيل، أنه جعل علم الله تعالى ذات الله تعالى كما جعل الذات علما، وهذا يتناقض، وهؤلاء أصحابنا لما جعلوا بقاء العلم نفس العلم جعلوا نفس العلم بقاء. وسنكشف عن حقيقة هذا الكلام إذا انتهينا إلى أجوبة شبهات الخصوم - إن شاء الله تعالى.
Eternal, but they say: Indeed God Most High is eternal by His attributes, so the objection does not apply to them. And among them are those who say this name is an affirmation of eternity, except that His knowledge Most High is eternal by an eternity subsisting in the essence of the Creator. These too have fulfilled the obligation of answering the objection. And among them are those who say: He is eternal by an eternity that is He and His essence, so it became a designation for the essence being eternal in itself, and this is not corrupt, but rather it corrupts the doctrine of Abu al-Hudhayl, that he made God's knowledge the essence of God Most High just as he made the essence knowledge, and this is contradictory. And our companions, when they made the eternity of knowledge the same as knowledge, they made knowledge itself eternity. And we will uncover the reality of this speech when we reach answers to the doubts of the opponents - God willing.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents three different opinions regarding how God's attribute of baqa' (eternity) is understood, responding to an implied objection that if God is eternal by an attribute, that attribute would be eternal as well. The first group says God is eternal by His attributes, so no objection arises. The second group says the name 'eternal' proves eternity, but God's knowledge is eternal by an eternity subsisting in the divine essence, thus also avoiding the problem. The third group says God is eternal by an eternity identical to His essence, so eternity is not a separate attribute. Nasafi then critiques Abu al-Hudhayl's view for confusing the essence and attributes, and notes that the Maturidi scholars (أصحابنا) consider the eternity of knowledge to be identical to knowledge itself. He promises to clarify this further later.

Correction: The passage shows that it is an eternal attribute subsisting in the essence, and in the third opinion it is identical to the essence itself.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position affirms that God's eternity is not a separate attribute but is identical to His essence, avoiding the pitfalls of Abu al-Hudhayl's view.

Refuting the Metaphorical Analogy from the Quran
Nasafi rejects the metaphorical analogy from the Quran's description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi rejects the analogical argument based on the metaphorical description of the Quran as 'Majid'.

Core Idea
Metaphorical usage in scripture does not apply to real divine attributes.

Nasafi begins by addressing a potential objection: if the Quran can be called 'Majid' without majesty being a real attribute in it, why can't God be said to know by His essence? He distinguishes between metaphorical and real attributes. This lesson explains that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening sentence where the objection is raised: that God called the Quran Majid though majesty is not a meaning subsisting in it.
Notice the phrase 'استحالة قيام الصفة بالصفة'.
2
Understand Nasafi's answer
Nasafi says that 'Majid' is used metaphorically, not literally. Metaphorical expressions are not in dispute.
3
See the application
He concludes that in the case of divine attributes, the matter is different; hence the analogy fails.
This sets up the main argument to follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال إن الله تعالى وصف القرآن بأنه مجيد وإن لم يكن المجد معنى قائما به لاستحالة قيام الصفة بالصفة، لأنا نقول : أراد بالمجيد المجد مجازا، لا مجد لمن يمسك به، ولا كلام في الألفاظ المجازية. وفيما نحن فيه الأمر بخلافه. ثم يقال لهم: الموافقة بين ذات الله تعالى وبين العلوم أكثر ، أم الموافقة بين العلم والجهل؟ فإن قالوا: بين ذاته وبين العلوم أحالوا ، لأن العلم والجهل كل واحد منهما عرض مستحيل البقاء، ومفترق إلى محل وكل واحد منهما في الشاهد من جنس الضمائر، ولا موافقة بين ذات الله تعالى وبين العلم بوجه من الوجوه. ثم لما استحال أن يعلم بالجهل، وتصير الذات به عالمة فلأن يستحيل أن يعلم بذات الله وتصير الذات به عالمة أولى. ولا يقال : إن بين علم الباري وبين علومنا مخالفة ومع ذلك جاز لكم أن تقولوا: إنه عالم بعلم، كما يعلم المحدث بالعلم، وإن لم يكن بين العلم القديم والعلم المحدث موافقة، فكذا جاز لنا أن نقول: إنه يعلم بذاته وإن لم يكن بين ذاته وبين العلم المحدث موافقة قلنا : قولكم: إن علمه مخالف للعلوم، كما أن ذاته تخالفها مناقضة، لأنه علم فلو خالف العلوم لخالف نفسه فإن قالوا: هل يخالف علمه العلوم المحدثة؟ قلنا من حيث إنه علم لم لا ، لأن حقيقة ما يستحق أن يسمى به علما كانت موجودة في الشاهد والغائب، ولا يجري فيها المخالفة، ولو جرت فيها المخالفة، لم يكن المباين المخالف للعلم فيما صار به علما مستحقا إطلاق اسم العلم عليه. فبطل هذا الإلزام وصح ما ألزمناهم يدل عليه أنه لو كان عالما لنفسه، وقادرا لنفسه لكان قادرا بما به يعلم وهو محال فاسد على ما قررنا في إبطال كلام أبي الهذيل. والله الموفق.
And it cannot be said that God Most High described the Quran as 'Majid' even though majesty is not a meaning subsisting in it due to the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in an attribute, for we say: By 'Majid' He meant majesty metaphorically, not majesty for one who holds it, and there is no argument about metaphorical expressions. But in the matter at hand, it is otherwise. Then it is said to them: Is the agreement between God's essence and the [various] knowledges greater, or the agreement between knowledge and ignorance? If they say: between His essence and knowledges, they commit an impossibility, because knowledge and ignorance are each accidents impossible of enduring, and they are divisible into a locus, and each of them in the witnessed realm is of the genus of inner states, and there is no agreement between God's essence and knowledge in any respect. Then since it is impossible that He knows by ignorance and the essence becomes knowing thereby, it is more fitting that it is impossible that He knows by God's essence and the essence becomes knowing thereby. And it cannot be said: There is a difference between the Creator's knowledge and our knowledges, and yet it is permissible for you to say: He is knowing by a knowledge, just as the contingent knows by knowledge, even if there is no agreement between the eternal knowledge and the contingent knowledge. Likewise it is permissible for us to say: He knows by His essence even if there is no agreement between His essence and contingent knowledge. We say: Your statement that His knowledge is different from knowledges, just as His essence is different from them, is contradictory, because it is knowledge, so if it differed from knowledges it would differ from itself. If they say: Is His knowledge different from contingent knowledges? We say: Insofar as it is knowledge, no, because the reality that deserves to be called knowledge existed in the witnessed and the absent, and difference does not occur in it. If difference occurred in it, then the thing that is distinct and different from knowledge in that by which it becomes knowledge would not deserve the application of the name 'knowledge'. So this objection is nullified and what we required of them is established, indicating that if He were knowing by Himself and powerful by Himself, then He would be powerful by that by which He knows, which is impossible and false, as we have established in invalidating the speech of Abu al-Hudhayl. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God knows by His essence. Nasafi begins by rejecting an analogy drawn from the metaphorical description of the Quran as 'Majid' (glorious), stating that metaphorical usage does not apply to the real attributes of God. He then presents an argument by dichotomy: asking whether there is more agreement between God's essence and knowledges, or between knowledge and ignorance. If they claim agreement between His essence and knowledges, they are refuted because knowledge and ignorance are both accidents and cannot be compared to the divine essence. Since it is impossible for God to know by ignorance, it is even more impossible for Him to know by His essence, because the essence would become knowing by something that is not an attribute. Nasafi then addresses a counterargument: that since divine knowledge is different from human knowledge, we can say God knows by knowledge, so why can't they say He knows by His essence despite difference? He responds that this is contradictory: if His knowledge differs from all knowledge, it would differ from itself because knowledge is a genus. Therefore, the analogy fails. He concludes that if God were knowing by Himself, He would also be powerful by Himself, leading to an absurdity where power and knowledge are conflated. The passage ends by affirming that the previous objection is invalidated.

Key Terms
مجيد
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That metaphorical usage in the Quran can be used to deny real divine attributes.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that metaphorical expressions do not negate the reality of attributes; they are a different category.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does the description of the Quran as 'Majid' not support the claim that God knows by His essence?
A) Because 'Majid' is a metaphorical expression, not a real attribute
B) Because the Quran is not an attribute of God
C) Because majesty cannot subsist in the Quran
D) Because the objector's argument is based on ignorance

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical interpretations of scripture do not undermine the reality of divine attributes; they belong to a separate category of language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's knowledge of your every action is real and not just a metaphor? How would your life change if you lived with that certainty starting today?

The Dichotomy of Agreement: Essence vs. Knowledge
Nasafi uses a dichotomy argument to refute knowledge by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that since there is no agreement between God's essence and knowledge, God cannot know by His essence.

Core Idea
If God's essence does not agree with knowledge, He cannot know by it.

Nasafi presents a powerful argument by dichotomy. He asks whether there is more agreement between God's essence and knowledges, or between knowledge and ignorance. He then shows that claiming agreement between essence and knowledge leads to absurdity, and that the impossibility of knowing by ignorance proves the impossibility of knowing by essence. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Read the dichotomy question: is the agreement between God's essence and knowledges greater, or between knowledge and ignorance?
This sets up a comparison.
2
Analyze the first option
If they say agreement between essence and knowledges, they commit an impossibility because knowledge and ignorance are both accidents (إعراض) that cannot be compared to the divine essence.
Accidents are impermanent and require a locus.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since it is impossible for God to know by ignorance, it is even more impossible for Him to know by His essence, because the essence would become knowing by something that is not an attribute.
The argument is a fortiori.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال إن الله تعالى وصف القرآن بأنه مجيد وإن لم يكن المجد معنى قائما به لاستحالة قيام الصفة بالصفة، لأنا نقول : أراد بالمجيد المجد مجازا، لا مجد لمن يمسك به، ولا كلام في الألفاظ المجازية. وفيما نحن فيه الأمر بخلافه. ثم يقال لهم: الموافقة بين ذات الله تعالى وبين العلوم أكثر ، أم الموافقة بين العلم والجهل؟ فإن قالوا: بين ذاته وبين العلوم أحالوا ، لأن العلم والجهل كل واحد منهما عرض مستحيل البقاء، ومفترق إلى محل وكل واحد منهما في الشاهد من جنس الضمائر، ولا موافقة بين ذات الله تعالى وبين العلم بوجه من الوجوه. ثم لما استحال أن يعلم بالجهل، وتصير الذات به عالمة فلأن يستحيل أن يعلم بذات الله وتصير الذات به عالمة أولى. ولا يقال : إن بين علم الباري وبين علومنا مخالفة ومع ذلك جاز لكم أن تقولوا: إنه عالم بعلم، كما يعلم المحدث بالعلم، وإن لم يكن بين العلم القديم والعلم المحدث موافقة، فكذا جاز لنا أن نقول: إنه يعلم بذاته وإن لم يكن بين ذاته وبين العلم المحدث موافقة قلنا : قولكم: إن علمه مخالف للعلوم، كما أن ذاته تخالفها مناقضة، لأنه علم فلو خالف العلوم لخالف نفسه فإن قالوا: هل يخالف علمه العلوم المحدثة؟ قلنا من حيث إنه علم لم لا ، لأن حقيقة ما يستحق أن يسمى به علما كانت موجودة في الشاهد والغائب، ولا يجري فيها المخالفة، ولو جرت فيها المخالفة، لم يكن المباين المخالف للعلم فيما صار به علما مستحقا إطلاق اسم العلم عليه. فبطل هذا الإلزام وصح ما ألزمناهم يدل عليه أنه لو كان عالما لنفسه، وقادرا لنفسه لكان قادرا بما به يعلم وهو محال فاسد على ما قررنا في إبطال كلام أبي الهذيل. والله الموفق.
And it cannot be said that God Most High described the Quran as 'Majid' even though majesty is not a meaning subsisting in it due to the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in an attribute, for we say: By 'Majid' He meant majesty metaphorically, not majesty for one who holds it, and there is no argument about metaphorical expressions. But in the matter at hand, it is otherwise. Then it is said to them: Is the agreement between God's essence and the [various] knowledges greater, or the agreement between knowledge and ignorance? If they say: between His essence and knowledges, they commit an impossibility, because knowledge and ignorance are each accidents impossible of enduring, and they are divisible into a locus, and each of them in the witnessed realm is of the genus of inner states, and there is no agreement between God's essence and knowledge in any respect. Then since it is impossible that He knows by ignorance and the essence becomes knowing thereby, it is more fitting that it is impossible that He knows by God's essence and the essence becomes knowing thereby. And it cannot be said: There is a difference between the Creator's knowledge and our knowledges, and yet it is permissible for you to say: He is knowing by a knowledge, just as the contingent knows by knowledge, even if there is no agreement between the eternal knowledge and the contingent knowledge. Likewise it is permissible for us to say: He knows by His essence even if there is no agreement between His essence and contingent knowledge. We say: Your statement that His knowledge is different from knowledges, just as His essence is different from them, is contradictory, because it is knowledge, so if it differed from knowledges it would differ from itself. If they say: Is His knowledge different from contingent knowledges? We say: Insofar as it is knowledge, no, because the reality that deserves to be called knowledge existed in the witnessed and the absent, and difference does not occur in it. If difference occurred in it, then the thing that is distinct and different from knowledge in that by which it becomes knowledge would not deserve the application of the name 'knowledge'. So this objection is nullified and what we required of them is established, indicating that if He were knowing by Himself and powerful by Himself, then He would be powerful by that by which He knows, which is impossible and false, as we have established in invalidating the speech of Abu al-Hudhayl. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God knows by His essence. Nasafi begins by rejecting an analogy drawn from the metaphorical description of the Quran as 'Majid' (glorious), stating that metaphorical usage does not apply to the real attributes of God. He then presents an argument by dichotomy: asking whether there is more agreement between God's essence and knowledges, or between knowledge and ignorance. If they claim agreement between His essence and knowledges, they are refuted because knowledge and ignorance are both accidents and cannot be compared to the divine essence. Since it is impossible for God to know by ignorance, it is even more impossible for Him to know by His essence, because the essence would become knowing by something that is not an attribute. Nasafi then addresses a counterargument: that since divine knowledge is different from human knowledge, we can say God knows by knowledge, so why can't they say He knows by His essence despite difference? He responds that this is contradictory: if His knowledge differs from all knowledge, it would differ from itself because knowledge is a genus. Therefore, the analogy fails. He concludes that if God were knowing by Himself, He would also be powerful by Himself, leading to an absurdity where power and knowledge are conflated. The passage ends by affirming that the previous objection is invalidated.

Key Terms
موافقة
عرض
ذات
علم
جهل
مخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Nasafi's argument that claiming agreement between God's essence and knowledge is impossible?
A) Because knowledge is an eternal attribute
B) Because knowledge and ignorance are both accidents
C) Because God's essence is unlike any attribute
D) Because knowledge is a genus of inner states

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of God knowing by ignorance demonstrates the impossibility of God knowing by His essence, by a fortiori reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's knowledge, do you instinctively compare it to human knowledge? This passage shatters any such comparison—are you willing to let go of that mental crutch and accept that His knowledge is utterly unlike ours?
?
If there is no agreement between Allah's essence and any created attribute, how does that challenge your tendency to project human qualities onto Him? What specific mental image of Allah do you need to dismantle today?
?
This argument forces you to choose: either Allah knows in a way beyond all analogy, or you reduce Him to creation. In what aspect of your worship—your prayers, your trust, your fear—are you still treating Allah as if He is understandable by human standards? Resolve to change that starting now.

Defending the Real Nature of Divine Knowledge
Nasafi defends the analogy by asserting the univocal nature of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's rebuttal to the counterargument that divine knowledge differs from human knowledge, and his conclusion about the impossibility of knowledge by essence.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge shares the essential reality of knowledge, so analogy holds.

The opponents might argue that since divine knowledge is different from human knowledge, we cannot use analogy. Nasafi responds that the essential nature of knowledge is the same in both cases; otherwise, it would not be called knowledge. He then concludes with a powerful argument that if God were knowing by Himself, He would be powerful by Himself, leading to a contradiction. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the counterargument
The opponents say that since divine knowledge is different, why can't they say God knows by His essence despite difference?
They try to turn the tables.
2
Understand Nasafi's response
Nasafi says that if divine knowledge differed from all knowledge, it would differ from itself because knowledge is a genus. So the analogy is valid.
This uses the concept of essential vs. accidental properties.
3
Follow the final conclusion
Nasafi concludes that if God were knowing by Himself, He would also be powerful by Himself, which is false because power and knowledge are distinct attributes.
This ties back to refuting Abu al-Hudhayl.

Defending the Univocity of Knowledge
Response to an objection about knowledge being created in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi theologians defend the univocity of the term 'knowledge' when applied to God and creation, and refute the objection that God's knowledge of temporal events would entail change in His essence.

Core Idea
The reality of knowledge is one; difference would break the meaning of the word.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla addresses a key objection from Abu al-Hudhayl: if God knows temporal things, does His knowledge change? The author argues that the reality of knowledge is the same whether in the witnessed or the unseen, and that difference would undermine the very term 'knowledge'. He then refutes a related claim about attributes being identical to the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical connectors like 'لأن' and 'فبطل'.
2
Identify the core argument
Identify the key claim: the reality of knowledge exists in both the witnessed and the unseen without difference. Ask yourself why this matters for the objection.
This is the heart of the defense against change in God's knowledge.
3
Examine the refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl
The author adds a separate argument: if God were knowing by Himself and powerful by Himself, He would be powerful by that by which He knows, which is absurd. Understand why this refutes a specific view.
This shows that knowledge and power are distinct attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علمه العلوم المحدثة؟ قلنا من حيث إنه علم لم لا، لأن حقيقة ما يستحق أن يسمى به علما كانت موجودة في الشاهد والغائب، ولا يجري فيها المخالفة، ولو جرت فيها المخالفة، لم يكن المباين المخالف للعلم فيما صار به علما مستحقا إطلاق اسم العلم عليه. فبطل هذا الإلزام وصح ما ألزمناهم يدل عليه أنه لو كان عالما لنفسه، وقادرا لنفسه لكان قادرا بما به يعلم وهو محال فاسد على ما قررنا في إبطال كلام أبي الهذيل. والله الموفق.
[Does He] know created sciences? We say: insofar as it is knowledge, why not? Because the reality that deserves to be called knowledge exists in the witnessed and the unseen, and difference does not occur in it. If difference occurred in it, then that which is distinct and differing from knowledge in that by which it becomes knowledge would not deserve the name 'knowledge'. So this objection is invalid, and what we objected to them stands. Evidence for it is that if He were knowing by Himself and powerful by Himself, then He would be powerful by that by which He knows, which is absurd and corrupt according to what we established in refuting the speech of Abu al-Hudhayl. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection about God's knowledge of created things. The author argues that the reality of knowledge is one and the same whether in the witnessed world or in the unseen (God). If it were different, then the term 'knowledge' could not be applied to both. He then refutes an earlier claim (by Abu al-Hudhayl) that if God were knowing by His essence and powerful by His essence, He would be powerful by the same attribute by which He knows, which is absurd. The argument shows that knowledge and power are distinct attributes.

Key Terms
علم
شاهد
غائب
ذات
أبو الهذيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection assumes that God's knowledge of temporal things requires a change in His attribute, making His knowledge temporal.
Correction: The author shows that the reality of knowledge is the same for God and creation, so God's knowledge of temporal events does not entail change; it is the same unchanging reality.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the reality of knowledge in the witnessed and the unseen?
A) It differs in each realm.
B) It is the same in both.
C) It only applies to the unseen.
D) It is created in both.

Key Takeaway
The reality of knowledge is one and the same for God and creation; difference would break the univocity of the term, and attributes are distinct from the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's knowledge of your every thought and deed is as real and detailed as your own knowledge of tangible things? How would your daily choices change if you lived with the unshakeable conviction that His knowledge is perfect, timeless, and unchanging?
?
The passage argues that God's knowledge of temporal events does not cause change in Him. How does this truth confront your own tendency to project human limitations onto God, especially when you feel He is distant or unaware of your current struggles?
?
If the reality of knowledge is one, then God knows your sins with the same certainty He knows your righteous acts. Does this humble you to the point of sincere repentance, or do you still think you can hide something from the One whose knowledge admits no difference?

Abu Hashim's Theory of States (Ahwal)
Abu Hashim's objection and defense of his theory of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's argument that attributes like 'knowing' refer to a state (hal) that is neither identical to the essence nor a distinct meaning, and how he defends this paradoxical concept.

Core Idea
Attributes are states that are neither essence nor meaning, neither existent nor non-existent.

Abu Hashim's theory of states attempts to resolve the paradox of divine attributes. He argues that when we say 'knowing' of God, we affirm a state that distinguishes the knowing essence from a non-knowing one. This state, he claims, is unlike anything we normally conceive. In this passage, you will see his objection to an earlier proof and his defense of this puzzling concept.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read how Abu Hashim objects to the earlier proof by claiming that 'knowing' indicates a state (hal). Notice the list of attributes he gives: knowing, living, powerful, hearing, seeing.
He asserts that these attributes are affirmations of a state.
2
Examine his paradoxical definition
He defines the state as not essence, not other, not meaning, not existent, not non-existent, etc. Why would he say that? Consider his attempt to avoid both identity and separate entity.
This is a famous and controversial aspect of the theory.
3
Follow his argument division
He argues that 'knowing' must refer to essence or meaning, both impossible, so it must be a state. Trace the logic: if to essence, all essences would be knowing; if to meaning, all meanings would be knowing.
This division is used to validate the need for a third category: states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعترض على هذا الدليل بأن قال: إن قولنا عالم في الشاهد والغائب جميعا إثبات لحال يخالف بها الذات ذاتا ليس بعالم فالعالم عالم بتلك الحال، وكذا الحي والقادر والسميع والبصير ثم تجاهل وقال: تلك الحال ليست هي الذات، ولا غير الذات، ولا معنى وراء الذات، ولا هي موجودة، ولا معدومة، ولا معلومة ولا مجهولة، ولا مذكورة ولا لا مذكورة واستدل لتصحيح هذيانه، بأن قال: ليس يخلو قولنا عالم من أن يكون رجوعا إلى الذات أو إلى المعنى، فإن كان رجوعا إلى الذات وجب كونه عالما لكونه ذاتا، ووجب اشتراك الذوات فيه، وإن كان رجوعا إلى المعنى وجب أن يكون عالما لكونه معنى واشتركت فيه المعاني. هكذا حكي هذا التعليل عنه بعض أهل الكلام بهذا اللفظ.
He objected to this proof by saying: Our saying 'knowing' in the witnessed and the unseen is an affirmation of a state (hal) by which the essence differs from an essence that is not knowing; so the knower is knowing by that state. Similarly, for 'living', 'powerful', 'hearing', and 'seeing'. Then he feigned ignorance and said: That state is not the essence, nor other than the essence, nor a meaning beyond the essence; it is neither existent nor non-existent, neither known nor unknown, neither mentioned nor not mentioned. And he argued to validate his nonsense by saying: Our saying 'knowing' cannot escape being either a reference to the essence or to a meaning. If it is a reference to the essence, then it would necessarily be knowing by virtue of being an essence, and all essences would share in it. If it is a reference to a meaning, then it would necessarily be knowing by virtue of being a meaning, and all meanings would share in it. Thus is reported this reasoning from him by some of the people of kalam in these very words.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from Abu Hashim against an earlier proof. He argues that the term 'knowing' (when applied to God and creatures) indicates a state (hal) that distinguishes a knowing essence from a non-knowing essence. He then defines that state as something neither identical to the essence nor distinct, neither existent nor non-existent, etc. To defend this paradoxical definition, he uses a division: 'knowing' must refer either to the essence or to a meaning. If to the essence, then every essence would be knowing; if to a meaning, then every meaning would be knowing. Since this is false, he concludes that 'knowing' must refer to a state, which is his theory of ahwal (states). The author reports this from him.

Key Terms
حال
ذات
معنى
أبو هاشم
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes are either identical to the essence or are separate entities.
Correction: Abu Hashim proposes states as a third category that are neither identical to the essence nor separate meanings.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hashim, what does the term 'knowing' refer to?
A) The essence alone
B) A meaning
C) A state (hal)
D) Either essence or meaning

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim argues that divine attributes like 'knowing' are states (ahwal) that are neither essence nor meaning, introduced to avoid both identity and separate entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is all-knowing,' do you ever feel a tension between affirming a real attribute and avoiding making it a separate thing? How does Abu Hashim's paradoxical 'state' challenge you to think more deeply about what you mean by that phrase? Will you simply repeat the words, or will you wrestle with the theological reality behind them?
?
Abu Hashim openly admits his 'state' is neither existent nor non-existent, neither known nor unknown. Does this honesty trouble you, or does it liberate you from pretending you fully understand God's nature? What is your own response when your intellect reaches its limit in describing Allah? Do you accept the mystery, or do you feel compelled to explain it away?

Abu Hashim's Induction Refuted: Condition for Valid Induction
Imam al-Nasafi refutes Abu Hashim's inductive argument for states by exposing its violation of the condition for valid induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hashim's inductive argument for states (ahwal) is invalid according to Imam al-Nasafi, and the correct condition for a valid induction.

Core Idea
A valid induction requires a merit; if all options are equally impossible, picking one is arbitrary.

Imam al-Nasafi presents Abu Hashim's argument for the theory of states, then exposes its fatal flaw: Abu Hashim fails to meet the condition for a sound induction. The passage is a masterclass in logical refutation, showing that without a principled reason to choose one division over others, the argument collapses into arbitrary judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Hashim's Argument
Read Abu Hashim's own definition of the 'hal' (state) and his inductive argument that 'knowing' must refer either to essence or meaning, leading him to posit a third option (the state). Notice the three divisions he considers.
Abu Hashim defines the state as neither existent nor non-existent, etc.
2
Identify the Three Divisions
The argument reduces to three divisions: (1) referring to essence, (2) referring to meaning, (3) referring to a state. Abu Hashim rejects the first two and chooses the third.
He argues that the first two lead to undesirable consequences.
3
Learn the Condition for Valid Induction
Al-Nasafi explains that for a valid induction, the chosen division must have a merit over the others—either by possibility (if others are impossible) or by obligation (if others are not obligatory). If all divisions are equally impossible, selection is arbitrary.
This condition prevents arbitrary choice.
4
Apply the Condition to Abu Hashim's Case
Al-Nasafi shows that Abu Hashim did not demonstrate that his chosen division (the state) is possible. Moreover, the impossibility of the state is obvious (badiha). His rejection of the other two divisions, even if granted, does not give his own view any merit.
Thus his induction is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تجاهل وقال : تلك الحال ليست هي الذات، ولا غير الذات، ولا معنى وراء الذات، ولا هي موجودة، ولا معدومة، ولا معلومة ولا مجهولة، ولا مذكورة ولا لا مذكورة. واستدل لتصحيح هذيانه، بأن قال: ليس يخلو قولنا عالم من أن يكون رجوعا إلى الذات أو إلى المعنى، فإن كان رجوعا إلى الذات وجب كونه عالما لكونه ذاتا، ووجب اشتراك الذوات فيه، وإن كان رجوعا إلى المعنى وجب أن يكون عالما لكونه معنى واشتركت فيه المعاني. هكذا حكي هذا التعليل عنه بعض أهل الكلام بهذا اللفظ.

إبطال كلام أبي هاشم: إن هذا النوع من التعليل صادر عن الجهل بشرط صحة الاستقراء وبيانه: أن شرط صحة الاستقراء وتعين بعض الأقسام لتعليق الحكم به أو للحكم بصحته، أن يكون لهذا القسم على غيره من الأقسام مزية وخصوصيته، إما بالإمكان إن كانت الأقسام الأخر ممتنعة بأن يقال: هذا يتنوع إلى كذا وكذا وكذا، فالأول ممتنع أن يدون أو يتعلق به الحكم، وكذا الثاني فتعين الثالث ضرورة، لإمكان ثبوته أو كونه علة للحكم. وإما بالوجوب بأن يقال: هذا يتنوع إلى كذا وكذا وكذا ولا وجوب للأول والثاني، والثالث واجب أو واجب أن يكون علة، فأما إذا استوت الأقسام كلها في الامتناع فتعين البعض للثبوت أو تعليق الحكم به، وإبطال الأقسام الأخر مع مساواتها الأول في المعنى جهل محض. وهو في هذا الكلام أبطل قسمين تصور عنده امتناعهما وعين الثالث، وهو القول بالأحوال من غير بيان إمكانه، بل امتناع ما ذكر من القسمين لو ساعدناه وجرينا معه على طريقة المسامحة كان ثابتا بالدليل، وامتناع ما ارتضاه مذهبا لنفسه، وعينه لعقيدته ثابت بالبديهة، بل هو بمحل لو صح، فلا أصح إذا من مذاهب المغالطية المتجاهلة من السوفسطائية من إثبات الواسطة بين الوجود والعدم والسلب والإيجاب كالمذكور ولا مذكور، وغير ذلك. فتعينه للثبوت مع امتناعه ورد غيره مع مساواته ما رده تحكم يقابله خصمه بمثله، ويقول: لما ثبت امتناع هذا القسم أعين قسما آخر سوى ما عينته، وكان خصمه أسعد حالا منه لخروجه عن التجاهل وإنكار الحقائق والعناد إلى الجهل والغلط، فعلم بما بينا جهله بشريطة الاستقراء. وبالله العصمة.
He then feigned ignorance and said: That state (al-hal) is not the essence, nor other than the essence, nor a meaning beyond the essence, nor is it existent, nor non-existent, nor known, nor unknown, nor mentioned nor not mentioned. And he argued to correct his nonsense, saying: Our statement 'knowing' cannot escape from being either a reference to the essence or to the meaning. If it is a reference to the essence, then it would necessitate that He is knowing by virtue of being an essence, and it would necessitate that essences share in it. If it is a reference to the meaning, then it would necessitate that He is knowing by virtue of being a meaning, and meanings would share in it. Thus this reasoning was narrated from him by some people of kalam in these words.

Refutation of the speech of Abu Hashim: This type of reasoning arises from ignorance of the condition for the validity of induction (al-istiqra') and its explanation: The condition for the validity of induction and the specification of some divisions for the attachment of the ruling or for judging its correctness is that this division have over the other divisions a merit and particularity—either by possibility (al-imkan) if the other divisions are impossible, such that it is said: This divides into such-and-such, so the first is impossible to be attached to the ruling, and likewise the second, so the third is necessarily specified due to the possibility of its existence or being the cause for the ruling. Or by obligation (al-wujub) such that it is said: This divides into such-and-such, and there is no obligation for the first and second, and the third is obligatory or must be the cause. But if all the divisions are equal in impossibility, then specifying some for existence or attaching the ruling to it, and invalidating the other divisions while they are equal to the first in meaning, is pure ignorance. In this speech, he invalidated two divisions whose impossibility he conceived, and specified the third, which is the affirmation of states (al-ahwal), without demonstrating its possibility. Rather, the impossibility of the mentioned two divisions—if we were to assist him and go along with him on the method of leniency—would be established by evidence, and the impossibility of what he adopted as his own madhhab and specified as his belief is established by intuition (al-badiha). Rather, it is at a level that, if it were valid, it would be among the madhhabs of the sophistical ignoramuses of the Sophists who affirm a mean between existence and non-existence, affirmation and negation, such as 'mentioned and not mentioned' and so on. So his specification of it for existence despite its impossibility, and his rejection of others while they are equal to what he rejected, is an arbitrary judgment (tahakkum) which his opponent would meet with a similar one and say: Since the impossibility of this division is established, I specify another division other than the one you specified. And his opponent would be in a better state than him because he escapes from feigned ignorance, denial of realities, and obstinacy to ignorance and error. Thus it is known from what we have shown his ignorance of the condition of induction. And with Allah is protection.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu Hashim's argument for the theory of 'ahwal' (states). It opens by quoting Abu Hashim's own definition of the 'hal' as something that is neither the essence, nor other than the essence, nor a meaning, and neither existent nor non-existent. He then presents Abu Hashim's inductive argument: the attribute 'knowing' must refer either to the essence or to a meaning; if it refers to the essence, all essences would be knowing; if to a meaning, all meanings would be knowing. Since this is false, Abu Hashim posits a third option—the 'hal'. The author (Imam al-Nasafi) then refutes this by explaining the proper condition for a valid induction (istiqra'). The condition is that the chosen division must have a merit over the others, either by possibility (when others are impossible) or by obligation (when others are not obligatory). If all divisions are equally impossible, then selecting one is arbitrary. Abu Hashim arbitrarily rejected two divisions (essence and meaning) and chose the third (states) without demonstrating its possibility. The impossibility of his own chosen view is actually obvious (badiha). Thus, his selection is mere tahakkum (arbitrary judgment). The passage concludes by stating that this exposes his ignorance of the condition of induction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Abu Hashim's error is that his third option (state) is impossible in itself.
Correction: Actually, the primary error is not that the state is impossible, but that his induction lacks a rational basis for preferring the state over the other divisions. Even if the other divisions were impossible, he did not show that the state is possible.

Checkpoint
What is the proper condition for a valid induction (istiqra') according to Imam al-Nasafi?
A) The chosen division must be the most commonly accepted.
B) The chosen division must have a merit over others, either by possibility or obligation.
C) The chosen division must be the one with the most proof texts.
D) The chosen division must be the one that avoids contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's theory of states is invalid because his inductive argument arbitrarily selects one division over others without a rational basis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief simply because the alternatives seemed weak, without honestly checking whether your own belief is actually possible or rationally grounded? How can you train yourself to apply the condition of valid induction—requiring a merit for your position—before you dismiss other views?
?
In what area of your faith or life have you been guilty of tahakkum—arbitrarily choosing a position without evidence, just because it seems convenient or familiar? What would it take for you to submit your own views to the same rigorous standard you demand of others?
?
If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow and He asked you: 'Why did you believe this about Me?' would you answer with a reasoned proof from the revelation, or with a borrowed opinion you never verified? How does this lesson call you to take ownership of your aqeedah?

The Parity Rebuttal Against Abu Hashim's Theory of States
Al-Nasafi uses a parity argument to show that Abu Hashim's theory of states leads to absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi uses a parity (mumathala) argument to expose an inconsistency in Abu Hashim's theory of states (ahwal).

Core Idea
Parity turns an opponent's logic against itself.

Imam al-Nasafi now turns to refute Abu Hashim's controversial theory of states. Rather than attacking from the outside, he uses a clever parity argument: he applies Abu Hashim's own reasoning to the theory itself, showing that it leads to absurdities. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the parity invitation
Read the opening lines where al-Nasafi sets up the parity method. Notice the phrase 'Take from your opponent like what you gave him'.
This is the key move: using the opponent's own standard against him.
2
Follow the absurd consequence
See how the parity leads to God being knowing and powerful by the same state, and even black by a state. Ask yourself why this is absurd.
The absurdity lies in attributing contradictory properties to a single state.
3
Trace the objections and replies
Identify Abu Hashim's possible objections (states not homogeneous, states are specific) and how the opponent responds using the same logic.
Al-Nasafi shows that the same logic applies to essences and meanings.
4
Understand the final dilemma
The opponent forces a choice about the state's reference: does it refer to essence or to a meaning? Either way undermines Abu Hashim's position.
If to essence, he rejoins the Ash'ari position; if to meaning, he contradicts himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نشتغل ببيان فساد كلامه فنقول له: خذ من خصمك مثل ما أعطيته، والتزم منه مثل ما التزمته فإنه يقابلك ويقول لك: لو لزمني إذا جعلته عالما لذاته أن أقول باشتراك الذوات أو إذا جعلته عالما لمعنى أن تشترك فيه المعاني. يلزمك إذا جعلته عالما بحال أن تشترك فيه الأحوال، فيصير عالما بالحال التي يصير بها قادرا وأسود بالحال التي يصير بها حيا، ولو قلت ليست الأحوال متجانسة. قيل لك: ليست الذوات متجانسة ولا المعاني متجانسة، فيجب بجميعها ما يجب بآحادها. فإن قلت: لا يصير عالما بحال مطلقة بل بحال يمتاز بها الذات عن ذات ليس بعالم يقال لك: لا يكون عالما بمطلق المعنى بل بمعنى هو علم يتبين به المعلوم على ما هو به ويتجلى، وعلى قول سلفك، لا يكون عالما بذات مطلق، بل بذات يمتاز به عن ذات ليس بعالم. ثم يقال لك: هذه الحالة راجعة إلى الذات أم إلى معنى وراء الذات؟ فإن هي راجعة إلى الذات فقد التحقت بسلفك، وإن قلت: هي راجعة إلى معنى قلت، هي
Then we occupy ourselves with explaining the corruption of his speech, and we say to him: Take from your opponent like what you gave him, and adhere to from him like what he adhered to. For he will confront you and say to you: If it were incumbent upon me, when I made Him knowing by His essence, to say that essences share in it, or when I made Him knowing by a meaning, that meanings share in it—then it would be incumbent upon you, when you make Him knowing by a state (hal), that states share in it. Thus He would become knowing by the state through which He becomes powerful, and black by the state through which He becomes living. If you say: States are not homogeneous, it will be said to you: Essences are not homogeneous, nor are meanings homogeneous, so that which is incumbent upon all of them is what is incumbent upon each individually. If you say: He does not become knowing by an absolute state, but by a state by which the essence is distinguished from an essence that is not knowing, it will be said to you: He is not knowing by an absolute meaning, but by a meaning which is knowledge by which the known is made manifest as it is and is revealed; and according to your predecessors, He is not knowing by an absolute essence, but by an essence distinguished from an essence that is not knowing. Then it will be said to you: This state—does it refer back to the essence or to a meaning beyond the essence? If it refers back to the essence, then you have joined your predecessors; and if you say it refers back to a meaning, then you have said...
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal by parity (mumathala) against Abu Hashim's theory of states. The author instructs the refuter to use the same methodology Abu Hashim employed. If Abu Hashim claims that making God knowing by essence implies sharing among essences, and making Him knowing by a meaning implies sharing among meanings, then by the same logic, making Him knowing by a state would imply sharing among states. This would lead to absurdities: e.g., God would be knowing by the same state through which He becomes powerful, or black through the state of being living. If Abu Hashim responds that states are not homogeneous (mutajanisa), the reply is that essences and meanings are also not homogeneous, so the same principle should apply. If he further restricts the state to a specific distinguishing state, the opponent can likewise restrict the meaning to a specific meaning (knowledge) and the essence to a specific divine essence. Finally, the opponent forces a dilemma: the state must either refer back to the essence or to a meaning. If to the essence, he rejoins the Ash'ari position; if to a meaning, he contradicts himself. The passage ends abruptly mid-sentence.

أحوال
ذات
معنى
متجانسة
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This rebuttal is a positive proof of the Ash'ari position.
Correction: It is not a positive proof but a demonstration of inconsistency in Abu Hashim's reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the main tactic Imam al-Nasafi uses against Abu Hashim in this passage?
A) He argues that states are impossible because they lead to infinite regress.
B) He uses a parity argument, showing that Abu Hashim's own logic forces absurd consequences.
C) He cites a consensus of the Salaf against the theory of states.
D) He argues that states are indistinguishable from accidents.

Key Takeaway
The parity argument exposes that Abu Hashim's theory of states would require accepting absurd consequences like God being knowing and powerful by the same state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your arguments with others, do you hold your own beliefs to the same standard of consistency that you demand from them? Think of a specific disagreement. What would it cost you to apply your own principle uniformly?
?
The refuser forces a dilemma: either the state refers to essence or meaning. Are there uncomfortable dilemmas in your faith that you avoid facing? What would it mean for you to confront one of them today, not just intellectually but with your whole heart?
?
This passage shows that a flawed premise leads to absurdity. Where in your life have you accepted a premise (about God, about yourself, about others) that, if consistently applied, would lead to an absurd or harmful conclusion? Will you uproot it now?
